## Cambuslang Golf Club

Founded in 1892, Cambuslang Golf Club sits on the south-eastern edge of Glasgow and offers a compact but characterful parkland test. The course measures just over 5,800 yards and is defined by tree-lined fairways, small greens and subtle elevation changes that place a premium on accuracy rather than power. Tight driving corridors mean the course rewards thoughtful positioning, and several holes play longer than their yardage due to gentle climbs.

The club has strong historical roots, with early course design input from local professionals before later refinements shaped the current layout. Although it has not hosted major professional championships, Cambuslang has long been respected in Lanarkshire golf circles and has produced a number of accomplished county players.

Its setting provides attractive views toward the Clyde Valley, and the routing flows naturally across rolling terrain. The closing stretch is particularly demanding, with approach shots requiring precision into well-guarded greens. Cambuslang's charm lies in its traditional feel—unpretentious, strategic and walkable—making it a favourite for golfers who appreciate classic club golf rather than modern resort presentation.

---

## Cameron House – The Carrick

The Carrick at Cameron House is a modern championship-style course set on the southern shores of Loch Lomond. Designed by Doug Carrick and opened in 2007, it stretches to over 7,000 yards and blends parkland and heathland characteristics with dramatic lochside holes.

The front nine winds through mature woodland and rising ground, demanding accurate driving and offering elevated views across the water. The back nine is more open and exposed, bringing wind into play and culminating in a memorable run of holes along Loch Lomond itself. The par-3 14th and par-4 18th are particularly striking, with the loch forming a constant visual and strategic presence.

While it has not hosted major professional events, the course has become one of Scotland's most recognisable resort layouts and a frequent venue for corporate and high-profile amateur competitions. Conditioning is typically excellent, and the expansive practice facilities add to its championship credentials.

The Carrick delivers a contrasting experience to traditional links—long, scenic and varied—yet still rooted in strategic design. Its combination of modern shaping, water hazards and natural elevation changes makes it both visually spectacular and a stern test for low-handicap golfers.

---

## Cameron House – The Wee Demon

The Wee Demon is a compact nine-hole course at Cameron House, also designed by Doug Carrick. Though short in length, it is far from a simple pitch-and-putt. Built to championship standards with immaculate conditioning, it emphasises precision iron play and imaginative short-game recovery.

The layout features cleverly contoured greens, strategically placed bunkers and several water hazards that demand accurate distance control. Holes vary in length and angle, encouraging players to use a full range of wedges and short irons. The closing hole, played toward the Cameron House complex, offers a scenic finish with subtle slopes that test putting nerve.

Primarily intended as an introduction to golf for resort guests and families, the Wee Demon has also become a popular warm-up course for those tackling the Carrick. Its design reflects modern short-course philosophy—fast to play, visually appealing and strategically engaging.

Despite its modest yardage, the course can challenge accomplished players while remaining accessible to beginners. The emphasis on shot-making and green reading makes it an ideal complement to the longer championship layout nearby, offering a relaxed yet thoughtfully designed golfing experience.

---

## Campsie Golf Club

Set on the lower slopes of the Campsie Fells, Campsie Golf Club offers a scenic moorland-parkland hybrid that enjoys panoramic views across the Kelvin Valley. The course plays just under 6,000 yards but features significant elevation changes, making club selection and course management crucial.

The layout moves naturally across undulating terrain, with several holes playing uphill into plateau greens that demand precise approaches. Tree-lined sections contrast with more open moorland holes where wind becomes a factor. The variety keeps the round engaging and visually rewarding.

Founded in 1898, the club reflects the character of traditional Scottish golf—modest in length yet strategically sound. While it has not hosted major championships, it has long been a respected venue for local competitions and county fixtures.

The closing holes are particularly memorable, with elevated tees providing sweeping views of the surrounding hills. Campsie's strength lies in its sense of place: a peaceful, rural setting combined with a thoughtful routing that maximises natural contours. It is a course that rewards patience, accurate iron play and an appreciation of Scotland's inland landscapes.

---

## Canmore Golf Club

Canmore Golf Club in Dunfermline is a classic parkland course laid out across gently rolling terrain. Established in 1914, it was influenced by prominent Scottish design principles of the era, with later refinements enhancing its strategic bunkering and green complexes.

The course measures around 6,400 yards and is known for its tree-lined fairways and well-protected greens. Accuracy from the tee is essential, as overhanging branches and subtle doglegs place a premium on positioning. The greens are typically fast and contoured, rewarding confident putting.

Canmore has hosted numerous regional amateur events and is highly regarded within Fife golf. While it lacks the coastal drama of nearby links, it compensates with immaculate conditioning and a strong emphasis on shot-making.

The layout builds steadily, with a demanding closing stretch that requires precise approaches into raised greens. Its welcoming atmosphere and strategic design make it popular with both members and visitors. Canmore exemplifies traditional Scottish parkland golf—challenging without being overly long, and rich in subtle design features that reward thoughtful play.

---

## Caprington Golf Club

Caprington Golf Club in Kilmarnock offers a picturesque parkland test set within mature woodland and rolling countryside. Founded in 1911, the course has evolved over time to incorporate modern bunkering while retaining its traditional character.

At just over 6,000 yards, Caprington emphasises accuracy and course management. Tree-lined fairways and natural water features come into play on several holes, and the greens are noted for their subtle slopes. The opening holes ease players into the round before the course tightens, demanding precise iron play.

The club has hosted Ayrshire county events and is well regarded for its friendly atmosphere and consistent conditioning. Its setting near Caprington Castle adds to the sense of history and place.

The closing stretch is particularly strong, with a challenging par-4 that requires a well-placed tee shot to set up an approach over a burn. Caprington's charm lies in its balance—playable for higher handicaps yet strategically engaging for better golfers. It is a fine example of inland Ayrshire golf, combining natural beauty with thoughtful design.

## Cardrona Golf Course

Cardrona Golf Course, near Peebles in the Scottish Borders, is a modern parkland layout designed by Dave Thomas. Opened in 2001, it stretches to over 6,800 yards and occupies a scenic position along the River Tweed.

Wide fairways and large greens give it a resort-style feel, but strategic bunkering and water hazards provide a solid test. The river comes into play on several holes, most notably on the closing stretch where risk-reward decisions are required.

Cardrona has hosted regional professional and amateur events and is known for its excellent practice facilities. The layout offers generous landing areas from the tee, making it accessible to higher handicaps, while the length and green complexes challenge low-handicap players.

The surrounding hills create a dramatic backdrop, and the course's modern shaping contrasts with the more traditional layouts found elsewhere in the Borders. Cardrona is a contemporary Scottish course that blends playability with championship aspirations, delivering a visually striking and enjoyable round.

---

## Cardross Golf Club

Cardross Golf Club, overlooking the River Clyde, is a scenic moorland-parkland course with sweeping views toward the Argyll hills. Founded in 1895, it has undergone several redesigns, most notably by James Braid, whose influence is evident in the strategic bunkering and elevated greens.

The course measures around 6,200 yards and is defined by its undulating terrain and exposed hillside holes where wind plays a significant role. The routing climbs steadily in the early stages before reaching higher ground that offers panoramic vistas.

Cardross has a strong reputation in Dunbartonshire golf and regularly hosts county competitions. The greens are a particular feature—small, firm and subtly contoured, requiring accurate approaches.

The back nine is more open and demanding, with several long par-4s that test both distance and control. Cardross combines natural beauty with classic design principles, providing a rewarding challenge that feels both traditional and distinctive.

---

## Carluke Golf Club

Carluke Golf Club is a traditional inland course set across rolling farmland in South Lanarkshire. Established in 1908, it reflects early 20th-century Scottish design, with a straightforward routing that makes excellent use of natural contours.

Measuring just under 6,000 yards, the course relies on strategic bunkering and sloping greens rather than length for its defence. Several holes play uphill, adding effective yardage and testing club selection. Tree-lined fairways and occasional water hazards provide variety throughout the round.

Carluke has hosted numerous local and county events and is known for its welcoming atmosphere. The greens are typically well maintained and feature subtle breaks that reward careful reading.

The closing holes form a strong finish, with a demanding par-4 requiring both accuracy and distance. Carluke's appeal lies in its honest, traditional challenge—fair, enjoyable and rooted in the character of Scottish club golf.

---

## Carnegie Links – Skibo Castle

Carnegie Links at Skibo Castle is a private championship course set within the grounds of the exclusive Skibo estate in the Highlands. Designed by Tom Watson, it opened in 2006 and blends links-style shaping with inland terrain.

The course stretches to over 7,000 yards and features wide fairways, large undulating greens and deep revetted bunkers. While technically inland, its sandy soil and fescue grasses create a links-like playing experience. Several holes run alongside the Dornoch Firth, offering spectacular coastal views.

Skibo is owned by the Carnegie Club, whose members have included high-profile figures from business and entertainment. The course is maintained to an exceptional standard and has hosted exclusive member tournaments and corporate events.

The design encourages strategic shot-making, with multiple angles into greens and risk-reward options from the tee. Carnegie Links combines luxury surroundings with a thoughtful, modern interpretation of links golf, making it one of Scotland's most distinctive private courses.

---

## Carnoustie – Championship Course

The Championship Course at Carnoustie is one of the world's most revered links, famed for its uncompromising difficulty. Originally laid out by Old Tom Morris and later refined by James Braid, it stretches to over 7,400 yards and is renowned for its narrow fairways, penal bunkering and the winding Barry Burn.

Carnoustie has hosted multiple Open Championships, including the dramatic 1999 victory of Paul Lawrie and the famous collapse of Jean van de Velde at the 72nd hole. It also staged the 2018 Open won by Francesco Molinari.

Every hole demands precision, with little margin for error. The closing stretch from the 14th onward is among the most demanding in championship golf, culminating in the iconic par-4 18th, where the Barry Burn threatens any approach.

Carnoustie's reputation as “Carnasty” reflects its relentless test. Firm fairways, deep bunkers and ever-present wind combine to challenge even the world's best players. It is a pure, traditional links that rewards accuracy, patience and strategic thinking.

---

## Carnoustie – Buddon Course

The Buddon Course at Carnoustie provides a contrasting experience to the Championship layout. Originally designed by James Braid, it is shorter and more forgiving, measuring just over 6,100 yards.

Wider fairways and fewer penal hazards make it more accessible, yet it retains a links character with firm turf and coastal winds. Several holes run parallel to the Championship Course, offering similar vistas without the same severity.

The Buddon is popular for visiting golfers seeking a Carnoustie experience at a more playable level. It has hosted numerous amateur events and qualifying competitions.

Strategic bunkering and well-placed greens ensure it remains engaging for skilled players, while its forgiving nature makes it enjoyable for higher handicaps. It serves as an excellent introduction to links golf and complements the more demanding Championship Course.

---

## Carnoustie – Burnside Course

The Burnside Course, also associated with James Braid, is the shortest of the three Carnoustie layouts but rich in character. Measuring under 6,000 yards, it offers a traditional links challenge with tight fairways and well-defended greens.

The Barry Burn comes into play on several holes, adding strategic interest. Despite its modest length, the course demands accuracy from the tee and precise iron play. It has hosted Open Championship qualifying in the past, highlighting its quality.

The Burnside's intimate scale and classic design make it a favourite among purists. It provides a stern test for low-handicap players while remaining manageable for others. As part of the Carnoustie complex, it offers a historic and authentic links experience.

---

## Carnwath Golf Club

Carnwath Golf Club is a traditional inland course in South Lanarkshire, founded in 1907. The layout spans gently undulating farmland and measures just under 6,000 yards.

Tree-lined fairways and small greens place emphasis on accuracy and short-game skill. Several holes feature subtle doglegs and well-placed bunkers that reward strategic positioning. The course has hosted local and county competitions and is known for its friendly atmosphere.

Carnwath's strength lies in its playability and traditional character. It offers a fair challenge for all abilities while maintaining the feel of classic Scottish club golf.

## Carradale Golf Club

The course is short but strategic, with small greens and natural hazards. Spectacular views of Arran enhance the experience, and the peaceful setting gives it a remote, traditional feel.

Carradale embodies the spirit of rural Scottish links golf—simple, scenic and rooted in natural landscape.

Carrbridge is known for its immaculate conditioning and welcoming club culture, offering a memorable Highland golfing experience.

## Cathcart Castle Golf Club

Cathcart Castle Golf Club, founded in 1895, is a historic parkland course on Glasgow's south side. The layout incorporates mature woodland, rolling terrain and strategically placed bunkers.

The club has hosted numerous regional events and is known for its strong competitive history. The course demands accuracy from the tee and precise iron play into well-guarded greens.

Cathcart Castle combines tradition, strategic design and a welcoming atmosphere, making it one of Glasgow's most respected member clubs.

Cathkin Braes Golf Club, established in 1888, holds the distinction of being the oldest golf course in Glasgow still on its original site. Its foundation began when three friends saw potential in the rolling moorland around Carmunnock, transforming farmland into a rudimentary 9‑hole layout that would evolve over the next century. The club expanded to a full 18 holes in 1906 and later invited famed architect James Braid to make improvements in the 1920s — his influence is still evident in the strategic bunkering and routing. ([cathkinbraesgolfclub.co.uk][1])

The course itself plays just over 6,200 yards from the back tees, a par‑71 moorland test that is relatively flat but deceptively tricky. Tree‑lined fairways flank many holes, and the par‑3 fifth requires an intimidating carry over water to a well‑protected green. Wide panoramic views stretch north toward the Campsie Hills and the Highlands, with glimpses of Arran on a clear day. ([VisitScotland][2])

Beyond history and scenic charm, Cathkin Braes boasts a proud tradition of fostering talent, producing successful amateur competitors over the decades. The clubhouse today offers welcoming facilities, blending rich heritage with a vibrant modern club spirit. ([cathkinbraesgolfclub.co.uk][1])

---

The Championship Course at Cawder Golf Club is a classic parkland layout just north of Glasgow, renowned for its James Braid design pedigree and rich golfing tradition. Designed by five‑time Open Champion and celebrated architect James Braid, this 18‑hole course stretches about 6,280 yards and plays to a par of 70 from the white tees — a length that demands thoughtful strategy over brute distance. ([Cawder Golf Club][3])

Set on the expansive Cawder Estate alongside the River Kelvin and the ancient route of the Antonine Wall, the Championship Course blends mature tree‑lined fairways with sweeping parkland vistas toward the Campsie Hills. Braid's influence is clear in the well‑protected greens and strategic bunkering; the stretch from holes 13 to 16 is even affectionately nicknamed the course's own “Amen Corner.” ([Cawder Golf Club][3])

Cawder has hosted serious competition too — the Championship layout was the venue for the Bowring Ladies Scottish Open in 1987 and 1988, adding a notable chapter to its competitive history. ([Wikipedia][4])

Visitors will also appreciate the elegant 18th‑century clubhouse, replete with original features overlooking the closing green, making the end of your round as memorable as the test itself. ([mygolfdays][5])

---

The Keir Course at Cawder Golf Club offers a contrasting round to its Championship sibling, combining a slightly shorter layout with thoughtful strategy and picturesque views over the surrounding countryside. Also influenced by James Braid, this par‑68 parkland course measures about 5,870 yards, making it a favourite for friendly rounds and relaxed society play. ([Cawder Golf Club][3])

Unlike many parkland courses that rely solely on length, Keir rewards precision and intelligent thinking. Its well‑bunkered greens and gentle elevation changes require accurate play from tee to green, and the routing near the River Kelvin creates a notably peaceful playing experience. Though shorter, the course doesn't feel insubstantial — small hazards and subtle contours ensure every shot matters. ([experiencegiftscotland.com][6])

The historic clubhouse, an 18th‑century building with vaulted ceilings and classic architecture, overlooks the Keir's finishing hole. Many golfers appreciate finishing their round here, soaking in the views and relaxed atmosphere that defines this part of Cawder Golf Club. Whether you're warming up before the Championship course or enjoying a standalone round, Keir offers a delightful blend of charm, challenge and Scottish parkland character. ([experiencegiftscotland.com][6])

## Cawdor Castle Golf Course

Perched within the historic grounds of Cawdor Castle near Nairn in the Scottish Highlands, the Cawdor Castle Golf Course offers a relaxed and scenic 9‑hole parkland experience steeped in heritage. The course has been in play since the mid‑1970s, offering visitors a charming and approachable round framed by ancient woodlands and the castle's storied gardens — part of an estate that's been home to the Cawdor family for over 600 years. ([Cawdor Castle][1])

Despite its modest scale — roughly 1,400 yards at par 32 — the layout provides enjoyable variety for golfers of all levels. Narrower fairways and well‑positioned greens invite thoughtful shot shaping, while the tranquil Highland surroundings create a memorable atmosphere that blends nature and sporting tradition. The course's relatively short length makes it perfect as a warm‑up for longer rounds nearby or a relaxed game during a castle visit. ([VisitScotland][2])

Cawdor Castle itself is a major highlight: a medieval tower house built in the 14th century, embedded in Scottish history, legend and literature, and unique in retaining its connection to the original family. Playing here feels like stepping into that living heritage, with golf woven into centuries of Highland culture. ([Cawdor Castle][3])

## Charleton Golf Club

Set on the stunning East Neuk coastline of Fife near Colinsburgh, Charleton Golf Club blends classic parkland golf with exceptional coastal views over the Firth of Forth. The 18‑hole, par‑72 layout stretches just over 6,400 yards and is known for its broad fairways, well‑contoured greens and strategic bunkering that reward thoughtful planning over sheer length. ([golfinscotland.co.uk][4])

Opened in 1994 and designed by John Salves, Charleton stands out for the panoramic vistas it offers from many tees — particularly on elevated holes where the Forth estuary provides a constantly shifting backdrop. While not a traditional links, its parkland character incorporates enough variety to be engaging for all handicaps. ([Visit Fife Golf][5])

The surrounding estate, anchored by the historic Charleton House — a stately building with architectural roots reaching back to William Adam — adds a sense of place and heritage to the round. Golf here feels like part of the wider landscape, with panoramic seaside views complementing the course's natural contours. ([Charleton][6])

Visitors will find ample hospitality both at the clubhouse and within the broader estate, which includes lodges and outdoor amenities. Charleton continues to build its reputation as one of Fife's more enjoyable parkland tests — memorable as much for its setting as its golf. ([Charleton][6])

## Clober Golf Club

Nestled in the leafy suburb of Milngavie just outside Glasgow, Clober Golf Club offers an inviting parkland course that's both accessible and strategically interesting. The layout plays about 4,960 yards with a par of 66, making it shorter than many traditional courses but playing significantly above its yardage thanks to undulating fairways, interesting elevation changes and subtle green complexes. ([Clober Golf Club][7])

What sets Clober apart is its strong connection to golf history — the club's Honorary President is Sandy Lyle MBE, the celebrated Open and Masters champion whose uncle, George Lyle, was the course designer. This familial link to one of Scotland's most successful golfers of the modern era gives the club unique character and a proud place within local golfing circles. ([Clober Golf Club][7])

The routing at Clober is thoughtful, looping through mature woodland and open parkland that challenge accuracy off the tee and short iron precision into well‑guarded greens. Though compact, the course uses elevation and tight angles to keep each hole fresh and demanding. ([Clober Golf Club][7])

Club spirit is strong, with a welcoming atmosphere and social calendar that complements the testing yet enjoyable nature of the golf. Clober proves that a shorter course can still deliver strategic depth, natural beauty, and a true sense of club tradition. ([Clober Golf Club][7])

## Cluny Golf Course

Located in rural Fife on the grounds of the Cluny Estate, the Cluny Golf Course is a friendly and accessible nine‑hole golf facility that showcases the gentle natural beauty of the surrounding countryside. Designed with leisure golfers in mind, this par‑32 course is well suited to families, beginners and those seeking a relaxed round in scenic surroundings. ([Cluny Activities][8])

Cluny is less about championship aspirations and more about pure enjoyment of the game and the landscape. The compact layout weaves through undulating terrain with a focus on playability, encouraging golfers to enjoy every shot without the intimidation of longer championship layouts. The openness of the course allows panoramic views across the fields and distant hills, contributing to its peaceful, welcoming character. ([Cluny Activities][8])

Despite its simplicity, the course has strategic features — from gentle doglegs to thoughtfully placed bunkers and subtle green breaks — that will keep players engaged and wanting to return. Its rural Fife location also makes it a pleasant stop for countryside golf tours and casual weekend outings. ([Cluny Activities][8])

Cluny exemplifies the charm of Scottish golf at its most accessible — a low‑pressure, scenic round where enjoyment is the primary goal, supported by well‑maintained fairways and a friendly club community. ([Cluny Activities][8])

## Clydebank & District Golf Club

Clydebank & District Golf Club is a mature parkland layout on the outskirts of Clydebank, providing a traditional inland golf experience just a short drive from Glasgow. With a course rating around 68 and measuring just over 5,800 yards, it plays as a fair test for players of a wide range of handicaps — demanding accuracy and thoughtful course management. 

The routing here meanders through spacious parkland terrain, featuring tree‑lined fairways, well‑placed bunkers and subtle elevation changes that add interest without overwhelming the golfer. The course's green complexes are typically true and receptive, encouraging crisp approaches and careful putting. 

While Clydebank & District hasn't hosted high‑profile tournaments, it enjoys a loyal membership and active competition calendar — a hallmark of Scotland's strong club golf culture. The clubhouse acts as a social hub after play, offering hearty hospitality and a welcoming atmosphere. 

What makes this course particularly appealing is its accessibility for city golfers seeking a quick yet rewarding round close to Glasgow, without sacrificing the sense of quiet countryside that makes inland courses so enjoyable.

## Cochrane Castle Golf Club

Cochrane Castle Golf Club
[Web](https://cochranecastle.com/?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
Address: Scott Ave, Johnstone PA5 0HF, United Kingdom
Phone: +441505320146

Cochrane Castle Golf Club in Johnstone, Renfrewshire, is an established parkland course dating from 1895, set on the historic Craigston estate. Originally laid out by noted professional Charlie Hunter, who succeeded Old Tom Morris at Prestwick, the course blends classic Victorian golfing traditions with modern strategic design. Over the years the layout has been adapted and refined, including contributions from James Braid in the mid‑20th century, giving the course a blend of old‑school routing and strategic bunkering that still challenges golfers today.([ukgolffederation.com][1])

The 18‑hole, par‑71 layout stretches to just over 6,190 yards from the back tees, with elevated fairways offering broad views over the Campsie Fells and Ben Lomond. Mature trees, subtle elevation changes and well‑placed bunkers demand accuracy off the tee and thoughtful approaches into the greens. The greens themselves reward careful putting and reading — a hallmark of traditional Scottish parkland.([golfinscotland.co.uk][2])

Cochrane Castle hosts a busy open competition calendar throughout the season, including senior, mixed and scratch events that attract competitive club golfers from across central Scotland.([ukgolffederation.com][1]) The clubhouse, warm and welcoming, serves as a social hub where members and visitors reflect on rounds with views over the final greens. This blend of history, challenge and generous hospitality makes Cochrane Castle a respected test in the Glasgow area.

## Colonsay Golf Course – Isle of Colonsay

The golf course on the Isle of Colonsay is a unique Scottish links laid out on natural machair (short grass growing in sandy soil) on the west coast of this small Hebridean island. While the club's formal founding dates from around 1930, golf is believed to have been played on these windswept fairways as early as 1775, making it one of the oldest continuous golfing traditions in Scotland.([Isle of Colonsay][3])

The 18‑hole links measures a modest 4,752 yards from the championship tees and features a distinctive routing that blends seamlessly into its coastal environment. With no traditional bunkers, the course's only hazards are those created by nature — sheep scrapes, rabbit holes and burn crossings — which have given this course its character and charm.([Isle of Colonsay][3])

Two sandy Hebridean bays frame the western edge of the layout, offering dramatic views and a constant sense of place that is rare even among Scotland's finest seaside courses. Local club tournaments and the annual Colonsay Open All‑Comers event draw islanders and visitors alike for a day of relaxed competition amid stunning scenery.([Isle of Colonsay][3])

Colonsay's golf is as much about experience as score: expect unpredictable winds, an intimate sense of isolation and the opportunity to play a game in its most elemental form, surrounded by sea, sky and Shetland sheep.

---

## Colvend Golf Club

Colvend Golf Club
[Web](http://www.colvendgolfclub.co.uk/?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
Address: Sandyhills, Dalbeattie DG5 4PY, United Kingdom
Phone: +44 1556 630398

Situated on the Solway Coast overlooking sweeping views towards the Cumbrian Fells and the Isle of Man, Colvend Golf Club blends classic parkland beauty with strategic variety. The club was founded in 1905, originally as a nine‑hole course designed by the esteemed Willie Fernie. Later expansions and redesigns by architect Dave Thomas — and further refinements by John Souter in the late 1990s and 2000s — have developed Colvend into a challenging 18‑hole, par‑69 course that rewards both bold strategy and tactical precision.([colvendgolfclub.co.uk][4])

Colvend's routing takes golfers through rolling fairways, tree‑lined corridors and around several water features that can come into play on key holes. The original nine holes are noted for their small, well‑guarded greens, while the later additions bring additional length and subtle elevation changes.([colvendgolfclub.co.uk][4])

One of the standout holes is the par‑3 8th, where a precise tee shot over a natural hazard is required to find the receptive green — a true test of focus and short‑iron accuracy.([colvendgolfclub.co.uk][4]) The course's Scenic Solway location makes it particularly special on calm mornings when reflections of distant hills are cast across the water.

Colvend combines classic design pedigree with a welcoming club culture. Its competitive calendar and scenic marshals of dunes and firth views offer a memorable day of golf in south‑west Scotland.

## Cowdenbeath Golf Club

Cowdenbeath Golf Club in Fife is a modern parkland layout with roots in the late 20th century. The course was established after work began on an initial nine holes in 1988 on the old Dora Coal mine site, and the full 18‑hole facility opened in 1991. Expanded further, the full 18‑hole layout was officially opened in 1996 by Sir Michael Bonallack, the influential former Secretary of The Royal and Ancient Golf Club and champion of amateur golf.([Cowdenbeath Golf Club][5])

Today Cowdenbeath plays to about 6,207 yards with a par of 71 from the back tees, providing a balanced test that suits a wide range of golfers. Its fairways and greens are typical of well‑conditioned Fife parkland, requiring accuracy from the tee and thoughtful approaches to complex greens framed by strategic bunkers and woodland edges.([Cowdenbeath Golf Club][5])

Though not a historic links venue like some nearby Fife coast courses, Cowdenbeath's relatively recent creation and thoughtful design give it a distinct identity — a modern inland challenge rooted in central Scottish golfing tradition. Its competitive fixtures draw local and visiting golfers alike, with the clubhouse providing a relaxed social hub post‑round.([Cowdenbeath Golf Club][5])

As a member of the local golf community with modern amenities and welcoming foliage, Cowdenbeath demonstrates how contemporary courses complement Scotland's deep golfing heritage.

Perched above the scenic Firth of Clyde in Dunoon, Cowal Golf Club is a classic Scottish parkland course with historic roots. Established in 1891 and designed by legendary architect James S. Braid, it combines beautifully matured parkland terrain with outstanding water views over the Clyde estuary, the Isle of Bute and, on clear days, even Ailsa Craig.([CowalGolfClubDunoon][6])

Originally laid out as an 18‑hole course, Cowal today challenges golfers over approximately 6,000 yards with a par that tests thoughtful decision‑making more than sheer power. Its mature tree lines, raised tees and well‑conditioned greens reward precise driving and careful approaches — often with the breath of coastal winds to influence club choice.([Scotland Where Golf Began][7])

Throughout its history, Cowal has been a respected stop on Scotland's Tartan Tour, hosting the Western Ferries Pro‑Am and nurturing talented players, including Honorary Member Lorne Kelly, who represented Great Britain & Ireland in Walker Cup play.([CowalGolfClubDunoon][6])

The clubhouse overlooks the Clyde, offering panoramic views and a strong social atmosphere. Cowal exemplifies classic parkland architecture with Scottish flair — blending tradition, challenge, and impressive scenery. Whether you're visiting via ferry from Gourock or driving around the peninsula, it's a course that encapsulates Scottish golf's blend of natural splendor and strategic design.

## Corrie Golf Club

Nestled in the picturesque village of Sannox on the Isle of Arran, Corrie Golf Club is one of Scotland's charming nine‑hole courses, dating back to 1892. Set against the dramatic backdrop of Glen Sannox and sweeping coastal views, this small but characterful layout offers a unique golfing experience where rugged Highland scenery meets classic Scottish golfing tradition.([Corrie Golf Club][8])

Although compact — typically playing as a par‑62 over 18 holes using the nine‑hole routing twice — Corrie demands accuracy and imagination. Its greens are known for being small and tricky to read, and the variety of hole lengths keeps each loop fresh for returning players.([Corrie Golf Club][8]) The course's atmosphere is wonderfully relaxed; there's no strict tee booking and the dress code reflects the informal spirit of island golf. Many players enjoy the flexibility to turn up and play, especially during quieter mornings and late afternoons.([Corrie Golf Club][8])

The setting here is the star attraction: views across the sea and neighbouring islands, complemented by the rugged hills of Arran, make this a truly scenic round. Corrie is a course where golfers relish the sense of adventure and camaraderie that comes with playing Scottish golf in its purest, most welcoming form.

Although it has not hosted major professional championships, Covesea is beloved by locals and visitors alike for its relaxed links charm. ## Crail – Balcomie Links

Crail's Balcomie Links is one of the great classic East Neuk layouts near the historic fishing village of Crail. Designed by Old Tom Morris in the late 19th century, Balcomie is often celebrated as one of the purest representations of traditional Scottish links golf — open, windswept and shaped by the sea breeze off the Firth of Forth.

The course stretches just over 6,700 yards, with gently rolling fairways, deep dunes and firm surfaces that reward ground play and creative shot‑making. Several holes near the coastline — especially the par‑4 16th and par‑4 17th — offer dramatic views of the North Sea, while the double green shared by the 17th and 18th typifies the routing ingenuity of classic links design.

Balcomie has never hosted The Open, but it has a storied history within Scottish amateur golf and is revered on the “Links Trail” alongside nearby courses like Elie and St Andrews. Competitive club events and open tournaments attract golfers seeking authentic seaside links at its best.

The clubhouse overlooks the North Sea, and the overall experience feels timeless: firm fairways, coastal winds and strategic design reward skill, imagination and respect for links heritage — making Balcomie a must‑play link in Scotland.

---

## Crail – Craighead Links

Just across from Balcomie, Craighead Links offers a distinct but complementary links experience at Crail. Designed in the early 20th century and later refined, Craighead plays slightly shorter than its neighbour but with more undulating dunes and tighter corridors that demand shaping and control.

Craighead's routing is firm and fast, with fairways that encourage run‑up shots and greens that can be subtle and challenging to read. Holes like the par‑4 9th and par‑5 13th reward thoughtful routing and bold decision‑making, especially in gusty conditions where wind becomes a factor.

This course hasn't hosted national championships, but it's highly respected on the East Neuk links circuit and draws golfers keen to experience two contrasting links at the same club. The back nine, with a series of holes nestled amid low dunes and natural hollows, showcases Scotland's classic seaside terrain.

Like Balcomie, Craighead sits beside the North Sea, and views of sandhills and seascapes are constant companions. The clubhouse serves both courses, fostering a welcoming atmosphere where stories of waves, wind and well‑placed shots are shared long after play.

Holes like the par-4 8th and par-5 17th provide strategic options, rewarding careful shot-making. Douglas Park hosts regular local competitions and society days, contributing to its reputation as a friendly, community-oriented club.

The clubhouse offers welcoming hospitality and panoramic views over key holes. Douglas Park exemplifies inland Scottish parkland golf, blending natural beauty with thoughtful course design.

---

## Douglas Water Golf Club

Douglas Water Golf Club, in Lanarkshire, is a parkland course renowned for its mature trees, undulating fairways, and challenging green complexes. The 18-hole layout measures roughly 6,200 yards with a par of 70.

Signature holes include the par-3 5th and par-4 12th, which test accuracy and course management. The course hosts a variety of amateur competitions and society events, fostering a welcoming local golfing community.

The clubhouse provides a social hub with views over key holes. Douglas Water combines classic parkland architecture with natural beauty, offering a strategic and enjoyable experience for players of all levels.

---

## Downfield Golf Club

Downfield Golf Club, located in Dundee, is a historic inland parkland course founded in 1901. The 18-hole layout measures around 6,200 yards with a par of 70 and features mature trees, bunkers, and subtly contoured greens.

Key holes include the par-4 10th and par-5 18th, which require accurate drives and thoughtful approaches. Downfield hosts local competitions, society days, and regional amateur events, maintaining a strong community focus.

The clubhouse provides hospitality with panoramic views across the course. Downfield exemplifies traditional Scottish parkland golf, balancing strategic challenge, natural beauty, and welcoming club culture.

---

## Drumoig Golf Club

Drumoig Golf Club, in Aberdeenshire, is a small but scenic 9-hole course that can be played twice for a full 18. Set amid gently rolling countryside, it measures approximately 2,800 yards per loop and emphasizes accuracy and course management.

Signature holes include the par-3 5th and par-4 8th, requiring precise tee shots and thoughtful approaches. Drumoig is largely amateur-focused, hosting local competitions, society days, and junior events.

The clubhouse provides a warm and welcoming environment for members and visitors. Drumoig embodies the charm of smaller Scottish parkland courses: scenic, approachable, and community-oriented.

Perfect! Here's the next batch of Scottish golf courses in alphabetical order, continuing our single consolidated list (~200 words each):

## Drumpellier Golf Club

Drumpellier Golf Club, located near Coatbridge, is a classic parkland course founded in 1905. The 18-hole layout measures approximately 6,200 yards with a par of 71 and features tree-lined fairways, mature woodland, and well-protected greens.

Notable holes include the par-4 8th and par-5 17th, which test accuracy and strategic thinking from tee to green. Drumpellier hosts local amateur competitions, society days, and junior events, fostering a strong community spirit.

The clubhouse offers a welcoming social environment with panoramic views of the course. Drumpellier exemplifies traditional Scottish parkland golf: challenging, scenic, and community-focused, appealing to players of all abilities.

## Duddingston Golf Club

Duddingston Golf Club, located on the edge of Edinburgh, is a historic inland course dating back to 1895. The 18-hole layout measures around 6,000 yards with a par of 70, featuring tree-lined fairways, gently undulating terrain, and small, well-protected greens.

Signature holes include the par-3 5th, which requires precision, and the par-4 14th, demanding thoughtful club selection. Duddingston hosts local competitions, society days, and junior events, making it a hub for community golf in the Edinburgh area.

The clubhouse provides a welcoming atmosphere with views over key holes. Duddingston blends historic charm with strategic design, creating a rewarding Scottish parkland experience.

---

## Duff House Royal Golf Club

Duff House Royal, in Banff, is a prestigious links course designed by Old Tom Morris in 1890 and considered one of Scotland's finest seaside layouts. Stretching over 6,400 yards with a par of 71, it combines classic links challenges with spectacular coastal scenery.

The course features firm fairways, deep bunkers, and undulating greens. Signature holes include the par-4 8th, which demands precision from the tee, and the par-5 15th along the coast, offering risk-reward strategy. Duff House Royal has hosted Scottish Amateur Championships and other prominent events, attracting golfers from across Scotland and abroad.

The clubhouse overlooks the North Sea, offering hospitality that reflects the course's rich heritage. The combination of strategic design, natural beauty, and history makes Duff House Royal a must-play for serious links enthusiasts.

---

## Dufftown Golf Club

Dufftown Golf Club, in Moray, is a traditional parkland course set amidst rolling countryside. Established in the early 20th century, it features 18 holes measuring around 6,000 yards with a par of 70.

The course challenges golfers with tree-lined fairways, subtle elevation changes, and strategic bunkering. Notable holes include the par-3 6th and par-4 14th, which reward careful shot-making and course management.

Dufftown hosts local competitions, society days, and amateur tournaments, maintaining a strong community atmosphere. The clubhouse provides a welcoming space for relaxation and socializing. Its combination of scenic landscapes, challenging holes, and friendly environment exemplifies inland Scottish parkland golf.

## Dullatur – Carrickstone Golf Club

Dullatur – Carrickstone, near Cumbernauld, is a parkland course known for its mature trees, strategic layout, and gentle elevation changes. Playing approximately 6,000 yards with a par of 70, it rewards accurate drives and thoughtful approaches.

Key holes include the par-4 7th and par-5 16th, which challenge golfers with risk-reward options and require careful navigation around bunkers. Dullatur – Carrickstone hosts local amateur competitions and society days, attracting golfers from central Scotland.

The clubhouse provides a welcoming environment, with views across the course and a strong community spirit. This course represents traditional Scottish parkland golf: strategic, accessible, and scenic.

## Dullatur – Antonine Golf Club

Dullatur – Antonine, also near Cumbernauld, offers a contrasting parkland test to Carrickstone. Measuring approximately 6,100 yards with a par of 71, it features tree-lined fairways, gentle undulations, and strategic bunkering.

Notable holes include the par-4 10th, which rewards precise driving, and the finishing par-5 18th, offering risk-reward decisions into a well-protected green. Antonine hosts a variety of local competitions and society days, fostering an active golfing community.

The clubhouse overlooks the course and provides hospitality, making it ideal for visitors and members alike. Dullatur – Antonine blends classic Scottish parkland characteristics with accessibility and challenge for all levels.

## Dumbarnie Golf Club

Dumbarnie Golf Club, located near Fife, is a links-style course that combines scenic coastal views with strategic design. The layout measures around 6,200 yards with a par of 71, offering natural dunes, firm fairways, and small, challenging greens.

Holes like the par-4 12th and par-5 15th reward creative shot-making and careful navigation of natural hazards. Dumbarnie has a strong amateur and society event schedule, emphasizing accessibility and enjoyable play.

The clubhouse provides stunning views over the Firth of Forth and coastal surroundings. Dumbarnie exemplifies the charm of Scottish seaside golf: strategic, scenic, and authentic, with a relaxed atmosphere for players of all levels.

## Dumbarton Golf Club

Dumbarton Golf Club, near Glasgow, is a mature parkland course dating back to 1895. Measuring roughly 6,000 yards with a par of 70, it offers tree-lined fairways, strategically placed bunkers, and subtle green undulations.

The par-4 9th and par-5 17th are notable holes, demanding accurate tee shots and thoughtful approaches. Dumbarton hosts a strong schedule of local competitions, society days, and amateur events, fostering a welcoming golfing community.

The clubhouse provides hospitality and panoramic views over key holes. Dumbarton exemplifies classic inland Scottish parkland golf: accessible, strategic, and steeped in local tradition.

## Dumfries and County Golf Club

Dumfries and County Golf Club, in Dumfries, is a parkland course established in 1885, featuring 18 holes across rolling countryside. Playing around 6,100 yards with a par of 71, it combines strategic challenges with scenic beauty.

The course includes tree-lined fairways, water hazards, and subtle elevation changes, with holes like the par-4 10th and par-5 18th testing accuracy and decision-making. Dumfries and County hosts local competitions and society days, maintaining a vibrant amateur golf scene.

The clubhouse offers a welcoming social environment. The course represents classic Scottish parkland tradition, balancing strategic golf with natural beauty in southwest Scotland.

## Dumfries and Galloway Golf Club

Dumfries and Galloway Golf Club, near Dumfries, is a historic parkland course dating to 1882. It plays roughly 6,200 yards with a par of 70, featuring mature trees, strategic bunkering, and subtly contoured greens.

Key holes include the par-3 6th and par-4 16th, requiring precision and careful course management. The club hosts local competitions, society days, and amateur championships.

The clubhouse provides hospitality and a social hub for members and visitors. This course blends tradition, challenge, and scenic parkland surroundings, representing the best of inland Scottish golf.

## Dunaverty Golf Club

Dunaverty Golf Club, located on the Kintyre Peninsula, is a charming 9-hole course set amid scenic coastal and Highland surroundings. While modest in size, measuring roughly 2,700 yards for 9 holes (or 5,400 yards if played twice for a full 18), it offers a strategic and enjoyable challenge for golfers of all abilities.

The course combines gently undulating fairways, natural hazards, and mature trees, creating variety despite its shorter layout. Holes such as the par-3 4th and par-5 7th stand out, requiring precise tee shots and careful approaches to navigate bunkers and sloping greens. The design emphasizes accuracy and thoughtful course management over sheer distance, making it ideal for both beginners and experienced golfers seeking a tactical test.

Dunaverty maintains a strong local club culture, hosting amateur competitions, society days, and friendly inter-club matches. The clubhouse provides a welcoming environment, with views across the course and surrounding countryside.

What sets Dunaverty apart is its natural beauty and peaceful location: golfers often enjoy panoramic views of Kintyre hills and the nearby coastline. Though smaller than many Scottish courses, it delivers an authentic and rewarding inland parkland experience, reflecting the charm and community spirit of Scotland's smaller clubs.

## Dunbar Golf Club

Dunbar Golf Club, in East Lothian, is a celebrated links course designed by Tom Morris in the late 19th century. Stretching around 6,400 yards with a par of 70, it features undulating fairways, deep bunkers, and firm greens, all set against dramatic views of the North Sea.

The course is strategic in nature, requiring precise shot-making and careful consideration of coastal winds. Signature holes include the par-4 12th, which demands accuracy off the tee, and the par-5 17th, offering a risk-reward decision along the dunes. Dunbar has hosted regional amateur tournaments and is a popular stop for visiting links enthusiasts exploring the East Lothian coast.

The clubhouse offers panoramic views over the course and the sea, with a welcoming atmosphere. Dunbar exemplifies classic Scottish links, blending history, challenge, and natural beauty in a memorable golfing experience.

---

## Dunblane New Golf Club

Dunblane New Golf Club, near Stirling, is a mature parkland course offering a balanced mix of challenge and accessibility. Measuring around 6,100 yards with a par of 70, it features tree-lined fairways, strategically placed bunkers, and subtly contoured greens.

Key holes include the par-4 9th and par-5 18th, where precise drives and thoughtful approaches are rewarded. The course has a strong community focus, hosting local competitions, society days, and junior events.

The clubhouse provides hospitality and scenic views over the surrounding countryside. Dunblane New embodies classic Scottish parkland design, emphasizing accuracy, strategic thinking, and enjoyable play for golfers of all abilities.

---

## Dundas Parks Golf Club

Dundas Parks Golf Club, located near Falkirk, is a modern 18-hole parkland course set in well-maintained, gently rolling terrain. Measuring approximately 6,200 yards with a par of 71, it features tree-lined fairways, bunkers, and water hazards that provide both challenge and variety.

The par-4 10th and par-5 16th are standout holes, offering risk-reward opportunities and requiring thoughtful navigation. Dundas Parks hosts local competitions, society events, and junior golf programs, fostering a vibrant club culture.

The clubhouse offers welcoming hospitality and views over key holes. Dundas Parks exemplifies modern inland Scottish parkland golf: strategic, scenic, and accessible for players of all levels.

---

Dundonald Links
Dundonald Links is a modern masterpiece that feels as though it has been carved out of the Ayrshire coast for centuries. Though a course existed on this site as far back as 1911—designed by the 1883 Open Champion Willie Fernie—the layout as it stands today is the work of acclaimed architect Kyle Phillips. Phillips, known for his work at Kingsbarns, was commissioned in 2003 to create a championship-standard links that could hold its own against the historic giants of the region. He succeeded spectacularly, blending rugged undulations with the classic coastal aesthetic.


The course has quickly become a heavyweight on the professional circuit. It has hosted the Scottish Open on multiple occasions, most notably in 2017 when Rafa Cabrera-Bello took the title, as well as the Trust Golf Women's Scottish Open. Unlike many flat links, Dundonald features significant elevation changes and is framed by pockets of pine trees, giving it a unique visual identity. The resort recently underwent a £25 million upgrade, adding luxury lodges and a world-class clubhouse, ensuring that the off-course experience matches the quality of the pristine, gorse-lined fairways.


Dunecht House
Tucked away in the heart of Aberdeenshire, Dunecht House offers a truly exclusive and intimate golfing experience. This is a private nine-hole parkland gem located within the sprawling estate of Dunecht House, a magnificent 19th-century mansion. Founded in 1920, the club was originally intended for the enjoyment of the estate's owners and their guests, and it retains that "secret garden" atmosphere to this day. It is a course that rewards precision over power, winding through mature Scottish woodland that provides both shelter and a stern test of accuracy.


While many courses in the region shout for attention, Dunecht House whispers. It is a favorite among locals who appreciate its tranquil setting and the breathtaking views of the Aberdeenshire countryside. The layout has stood the test of time with very few changes, preserving the original vision of a gentleman's sporting retreat. Membership remains limited, which means those lucky enough to play here often feel as though they have the entire estate to themselves. It is the quintessential example of traditional Scottish parkland golf—unpretentious, naturally beautiful, and deeply rooted in its local history.


Dunfermline Golf Club (Pitfirrane)
The Dunfermline Golf Club, often referred to as Pitfirrane, is steeped in a level of history that few clubs can match. The club was founded in 1887, but it moved to its current home at Pitfirrane Estate in 1953. The centerpiece of the property is the spectacular Pitfirrane Castle, which serves as the clubhouse; parts of this Category A listed building date back to the 15th century. Walking into the 19th hole here feels more like entering a museum than a locker room, providing a sense of gravitas to every round.


The 18-hole parkland course was designed by the esteemed J.R. Stutt, a prolific Scottish architect who worked alongside James Braid. Stutt utilized the natural undulations of the Fife landscape to create a testing layout that features no fewer than five par-fives. The signature 18th hole, "Halkett's Hame" is a grueling 480-yard finish that requires nerves of steel to conclude a round. Notable for its impeccably manicured fairways and fast greens, the course has long been a venue for regional qualifying events and amateur championships, consistently proving that it remains a formidable test for golfers of all skill levels.

Dunkeld and Birnam
Highland Perthshire is home to some of the most scenic golf in the world, and Dunkeld and Birnam is arguably its crown jewel. The club's origins trace back to 1892, with the original nine holes laid out by the legendary Old Tom Morris. While the course moved to its current hillside location in 1922, the spirit of "The Grand Old Man of Golf" still permeates the atmosphere. The official opening of the new site was a grand affair, conducted by Her Grace, The Duchess of Atholl, cementing the club's prestigious local standing.

In 2000, the course was expanded to 18 holes, with the newer section running alongside the Loch of Lowes. This expansion added a parkland dimension to the original heathland character, creating a fascinating "game of two halves." The elevation changes provide spectacular vistas of the Tay Valley and the historic town of Dunkeld. It is a course where you are just as likely to spot an osprey or a deer as you are to make a birdie. The layout is quirky and charismatic, demanding creative shot-making rather than brute force, making it a must-visit for those who value character in course design.

Dunnikier Park
Dunnikier Park is a delightful parkland course situated on the northern outskirts of Kirkcaldy. Designed by the Paisley-based architect John Stutt and opened in 1963, the course was part of a post-war movement to provide high-quality golf to the local community. Stutt's design philosophy is evident here: he used the natural contours of the parkland to create a fair but demanding test. The course is famously well-wooded, with mature trees lining almost every fairway, requiring players to be strategic with their off-the-tee placement.

Over the years, Dunnikier has built a reputation for its friendly, inclusive atmosphere. While it hasn't hosted the major professional opens of its coastal neighbors, it is a frequent host of Fife Golf Association events and has nurtured many talented local amateurs. The layout is particularly known for its challenging finish, where the final few holes can easily ruin a good scorecard. It offers a peaceful escape from the nearby town, providing a lush, green sanctuary that feels far removed from the bustle of modern life. It is the kind of course that rewards the "thinking golfer" who knows when to take a risk and when to play safe.

Dunning
The village of Dunning is home to a quintessential nine-hole course that captures the heart of Scottish village golf. Established in 1906, the course is located in the shadow of the historic St. Serf's Church, where the 12th-century tower looms over the fairways. The layout was designed by the prolific Tom Dunn, an architect who was instrumental in the spread of golf across the UK in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. Dunn's signature style—functional, natural, and accessible—is perfectly preserved in this scenic Perthshire valley.

Dunning is unique because it features two burns (the Dunning Burn and the Dewar Burn) that meander through the property, coming into play on several holes. Despite being a nine-hole layout, the club offers 18 separate tee positions, allowing for a varied second loop. It is a course that emphasizes the "short game" with small, tricky greens that require a deft touch. Famous for its warm hospitality, the club is a favorite for those touring the Gleneagles area who want a quick, charming round that highlights the beauty of the Ochil Hills without the championship price tag.

Duns
Set in the rolling hills of the Scottish Borders, Duns Golf Club is a hidden gem that offers a peaceful, panoramic golfing experience. The club was founded in 1894, but the course was redesigned and extended to its current 18-hole layout in the 1920s by the legendary James Braid. Braid's influence is unmistakable, particularly in the way the course utilizes the natural slopes of Hardens Hill to create challenging lies and strategic bunkering. The elevation provides views that on a clear day stretch across the Merse to the Cheviot Hills in England.

Duns is often described as a "course of two halves." The lower holes are relatively flat and parkland in nature, while the upper holes transition into a more rugged, moorland environment. This variety keeps the golfer engaged from start to finish. One of the club's most famous honorary members was the legendary Formula 1 champion Jim Clark, who hailed from the area. The club maintains a memorial to him, and the sense of local pride is palpable. It is a welcoming, traditional club that provides a rigorous but fair test of golf in one of Scotland's most underrated regions.

Durness
Durness Golf Club is widely regarded as one of the most bucket-list-worthy nine-hole courses in the world. Located in the far northwest Highlands, it is the most north-westerly course on the British mainland. It was remarkably designed and built in 1988 by three local enthusiasts—Lachie Ross, Ian Morrison, and Francis Keith—who saw the potential in the stunning limestone machair land. The result is a course that looks like it was designed by nature itself, with dramatic cliff-top views of Balnakeil Bay and the North Atlantic Ocean.


The signature 9th hole (or 18th, if playing twice) is a heart-stopping par-three that requires a shot across a yawning Atlantic chasm to a green perched on the cliffs. It is frequently cited as one of the most extraordinary holes in golf. While it lacks the "famous architect" pedigree of other courses, its sheer beauty and isolation have made it a cult favorite among international golf travelers. The course is maintained by a small, dedicated team, and the "honesty box" for green fees adds to the feeling of being at the very edge of the world.

Easter Moffat
Located in Lanarkshire, Easter Moffat Golf Club is a grand parkland course with a history dating back to 1922. The club's focal point is its stunning clubhouse—a Grade B listed mansion built in 1838. The transition from the historic elegance of the house to the lush green fairways sets a sophisticated tone for the day. Originally a nine-hole course, it was extended to 18 holes following the Second World War, with the layout evolving over the decades into the polished, championship-style course it is today.

The course is known for its wide fairways and large, undulating greens, which are often cited as some of the best-conditioned in the county. It sits on a high plateau, offering views across the Monklands and towards the Campsie Fells. While it provides a stern test for the low-handicapper, its fair layout makes it accessible for the casual player as well. Easter Moffat has long been a pillar of the Lanarkshire golf scene, frequently hosting county championships and producing several notable amateur players who have gone on to represent Scotland on the national stage.

East Kilbride
East Kilbride Golf Club represents the modern face of Scottish parkland golf. While the club was founded in 1900, it moved to its current location at Nerston in the late 1960s to accommodate the development of the "New Town." The course was designed by the well-known architect Fred Hawtree, whose family firm is one of the oldest in the business. Hawtree's design at East Kilbride is a masterclass in modern parkland architecture, featuring strategic bunkering, water hazards, and a variety of tree species that have now matured to provide a true sense of enclosure.

One of the club's most famous former members was Jack McLean, a three-time Scottish Amateur Champion in the 1930s who also reached the final of the US Amateur. His legacy continues to inspire the competitive spirit of the club today. The course is a frequent host of West of Scotland PGA events and is renowned for its excellent drainage, meaning it remains playable when other nearby courses are struggling with the Scottish weather. With a sleek, modern clubhouse opened in 2011, it remains one of the most popular and professional clubs in the central belt.

East Renfrewshire
High on the moors above the village of Newton Mearns sits East Renfrewshire Golf Club, a course that breathes with the legacy of the "Great Triumvirate." Established in 1922, the layout was designed by the five-time Open Champion James Braid. Braid was a master of using natural hazards, and here he utilized the rugged moorland terrain, rocky outcrops, and sweeping elevation changes to create a course that feels far more remote than its proximity to Glasgow suggests. On a clear day, the views from the highest points of the course stretch across the Clyde Valley to the peaks of Ben Lomond and the Southern Highlands.

The club has a rich history of hosting prestigious events, including the Scottish Professional Championship and the Scottish Youths' Championship. Its most famous "member" in spirit is arguably Braid himself, as the course remains one of the purest examples of his design philosophy in the West of Scotland. The holes are characterized by their strategic demand; Braid's bunkering forces players to choose between the aggressive line and the safe play. With its lush, springy turf and challenging wind conditions, it offers a stern but fair test that has remained largely unchanged for over a century.

Edzell (Old Course)
Edzell Golf Club is often described as one of the most aesthetically pleasing inland courses in Scotland. Situated at the gateway to the Grampian Mountains, the course was originally laid out in 1895 by Bob Simpson, the legendary clubmaker from Elie. However, it was a redesign in the 1930s by James Braid that truly elevated Edzell to its current status. The "Old Course" is a sophisticated blend of parkland and heathland, framed by majestic Scots pines and silver birches that provide a vibrant backdrop through the changing seasons.


The club is famous for its immaculate conditioning and its historic "Arch" which marks the entrance to the village and the club. One of the most notable tournaments held here was the Scottish Girls' Championship, reflecting the club's long-standing commitment to nurturing young talent. The layout is renowned for its consistency, with greens that are famously true and fast. It is a course that requires precision rather than power, particularly on the stretch of holes that run alongside the West Water river, where the margin for error is slim but the rewards for a well-placed drive are immense.

Edzell - West Water
While the Old Course at Edzell takes much of the spotlight, the West Water course is a vital and distinct part of the club's identity. Opened in 2001, this 9-hole layout was designed by Graeme Webster, one of Scotland's most respected modern architects. Webster's task was to create a course that complemented the historic Old Course while offering a different kind of challenge. The West Water is shorter and perhaps more "player-friendly" for high handicappers, but it is by no means a pushover, featuring modern bunkering and undulating greens that require a sharp short game.

The course takes its name from the river that borders the property, and the sound of rushing water provides a serene soundtrack to the round. It is an ideal venue for those looking for a quicker game or for members wishing to sharpen their iron play before tackling the longer 18-hole layout. Despite its relatively young age, the West Water has matured quickly, with the planting of thousands of native trees helping it blend seamlessly into the ancient landscape of the Angus glens. It represents the club's forward-thinking approach, ensuring that golf at Edzell remains accessible and varied for all generations.

Elderslie
Elderslie Golf Club, located just outside Paisley, holds a unique place in golfing history as the home club of James Braid during the time he was designing some of his most famous layouts. Although Braid didn't design the original course (which was laid out by the club's first professional), he lived nearby and frequently offered his expertise on improvements. The course as it stands today is a lush, rolling parkland paradise that provides a surprisingly quiet escape from the industrial heartlands of Renfrewshire.

The club is perhaps most famous for the Elderslie Trophy, a prestigious amateur event that has seen many future professionals compete over its challenging 6,100 yards. The terrain is characterized by "whins" (gorse) and mature woodland, with several blind shots that require local knowledge—or at least a very trusty yardage book. The 6th hole, "The Monument" is a highlight, offering views of the Wallace Monument in nearby Elderslie, the birthplace of Scottish hero William Wallace. It is a friendly, traditional club that prides itself on a warm "19th hole" welcome and a course that is always presented in championship condition.

Elgin
Elgin Golf Club is widely considered the finest inland course in the north of Scotland. Located in the heart of "Whisky Country" the club moved to its current site at Hardhillock in 1906. The course was originally designed by Peter McArthur, but like many of the greats, it saw significant refinement by James Braid. It is a classic heathland layout, sitting on a bed of free-draining sandy soil that allows for firm, fast playing conditions year-round. The fairways are lined with thick gorse and heather, making a straight drive an absolute necessity.


Elgin has a long history of hosting top-tier amateur events, including the Northern Open and various Scottish Golf national championships. The club has produced a number of high-profile players, and the level of local competition is notoriously high. The signature par-three 5th hole, played over a valley to a plateau green, is one of the most photographed in Moray. After a round, many golfers take advantage of the club's proximity to some of Scotland's most famous distilleries, making Elgin a central pillar of any Highland golf tour that values tradition and a high-quality test of skill.

Elie (The Golf House Club)
To play Elie is to step back in time. Formally known as The Golf House Club, golf has been played on this stunning piece of Fife coastline since the 16th century. The course reached its current 18-hole form in 1895 with the help of Old Tom Morris and James Braid, the latter of whom was born in the adjacent village of Earlsferry. Elie is unique among world-class courses because it has no par-fives and only two par-threes; however, its collection of long, testing par-fours, many of which are played over blind ridges, makes it a formidable challenge.

The most famous feature of Elie is the submarine periscope located in the starter's hut. Because the opening tee shot is played over a hill, the starter uses the periscope (salvaged from HMS Excalibur) to ensure the fairway is clear. Famous members include Braid himself and the legendary Sir Michael Bonallack, one of the most decorated amateurs in history. The course is a pure links, with firm turf, hidden pot bunkers, and the ever-present wind from the Firth of Forth. It is a "member's club" in the truest sense, exuding an air of understated elegance and historical significance that few places can match.

Elie Sports Club
Located just inland from the famous links of the Golf House Club, the Elie Sports Club (formerly the Elie Relief Course) offers a more relaxed, community-focused golfing experience. This 9-hole course is the perfect antidote to the high-pressure environment of championship links golf. It was designed to provide a space for beginners, juniors, and families to enjoy the game in the same coastal air as its more famous neighbor but without the intimidating length or the periscope-guarded blind shots.

The Sports Club is part of a larger facility that includes tennis and bowling, making it a hub of activity in the East Neuk of Fife. Despite its shorter length, the course is beautifully maintained and features many of the same links characteristics—sandy soil and coastal breezes—that define Fife golf. It is a particularly popular spot for "warm-up" rounds or for golfers who want to enjoy a morning game before a afternoon of sightseeing in the historic fishing villages nearby. It serves as a vital nursery for the next generation of Scottish golfers, maintaining the inclusive spirit that has kept the game alive in Fife for centuries.

Elmwood
Elmwood Golf, situated near Cupar in Fife, is a fascinating modern addition to the Scottish golf scene. Opened in the mid-1990s, it was designed by Graeme Webster and serves a dual purpose: it is both a popular public pay-and-play course and a key training facility for SRUC (Scotland's Rural College). Because it is used to train the next generation of greenkeepers and golf managers, the course is often in "textbook" condition, serving as a living classroom for sustainable turf management and modern course design.

The layout is a modern parkland-style course that makes clever use of environmentally friendly features, such as wildflower meadows and wetlands, which provide a rich habitat for local wildlife. It is not an overly long course, but the bunkering is strategic, and the greens are known for being remarkably smooth and receptive. Elmwood has hosted various PGA pro-ams and regional qualifying events, earning a reputation as a high-quality facility that punches well above its weight. It is perhaps the best place in Fife to see the future of the Scottish golf industry in action while enjoying a fun, accessible round of golf.

Isle of Eriska
The Isle of Eriska offers what is arguably the most exclusive and picturesque short-course experience in the British Isles. Located on a private 300-acre island off the west coast of Scotland near Oban, the 9-hole course is part of the world-renowned Isle of Eriska Hotel and Spa. The course was designed by Howard Swan and is intended to provide a luxury recreational experience for guests of the estate. It is a "hidden" course in the most literal sense, accessible only by a private bridge that connects the island to the mainland.

The holes wind through ancient woodland and along the rugged shoreline, offering views of Loch Linnhe and the Morvern mountains that are nothing short of cinematic. While it is a shorter course, the island's terrain provides natural defenses, with rocky outcrops and the sea breeze adding to the difficulty. It is a favorite haunt of celebrities and high-profile guests seeking total privacy. Playing here is less about the scorecard and more about the atmosphere—the scent of wild garlic in the air, the sight of golden eagles overhead, and the absolute silence that comes with playing on your own private island.

Erskine
Erskine Golf Club is a powerhouse of West of Scotland golf, located on the banks of the River Clyde with views across to the Kilpatrick Hills. Founded in 1904, the course was redesigned in the late 1920s by the legendary duo of James Braid and Willie Gaudin. The result is a premier parkland course that blends traditional design with the scale and drama of the Clyde estuary. The course is famously divided by the Bishopton-to-Erskine road, with the holes on either side offering slightly different characters—one more wooded and sheltered, the other more open to the elements.

The club has a storied history of hosting major championships, including the Scottish Professional Championship and the British Seniors' Championship. Its quality is such that it has long been used as a final qualifying venue for the Open Championship when it is held at nearby Royal Troon or Prestwick. Famous members have included several titans of Scottish industry, and the club maintains a high standard of etiquette and tradition. With its massive mature trees, deep bunkers, and slick greens, Erskine remains one of the most respected and physically demanding parkland tests in the country.

Eyemouth
Eyemouth Golf Club offers a heart-pounding experience on the Berwickshire coast that rivals the most famous cliff-top courses in the world. Originally a 9-hole course founded in 1894, it was extended to 18 holes in 1997 by architect William Swan. The modern layout is a spectacular hybrid of rolling parkland and "extreme" coastal golf. It is most famous for its 6th hole, "A' v weel" which was once voted Britain's most extraordinary golf hole. This par-three requires a daunting 160-plus yard carry over a deep, churning Atlantic inlet known as "Gull's Nest" to a green perched on a jagged promontory.

The course is a regular host of the Scottish Borders Open and is a favorite for visitors seeking a visual spectacle. Beyond the terrifying carries over the sea, the course offers panoramic views that stretch toward the Bass Rock in the north and Holy Island in the south. The wind is the primary defender here; on a calm day, the course is a delight of manicured turf, but when the North Sea gales blow, it becomes a survival test. It is a bold, modern links-style experience that emphasizes the raw, rugged beauty of Scotland's eastern coastline.

Fairmont St Andrews - Kittocks
The Kittocks course at the Fairmont St Andrews resort is a sprawling, modern masterpiece that commands the clifftops just outside the "Home of Golf." Originally a collaboration between Bruce Devlin and the legendary Gene Sarazen, the course was later refined by Gary Stephenson. It is named after the Kittocks Den, a natural ravine that meanders through the property. The design is American in scale—featuring large, undulating greens and expansive bunkers—but Scottish in soul, with fescue grasses and breathtaking views of the St Andrews skyline and the Eden Estuary.

Kittocks has hosted several prestigious events, including the Scottish Senior Open and EuroPro Tour events. One of its most famous features is the double green shared by the 14th and 17th holes, a nod to the historic Old Course nearby. The closing stretch is particularly dramatic, with the 17th hole running precariously along the very edge of the cliffs. It offers a more rugged, "wilder" feel than its sister course, the Torrance, and provides a stern test of ball-striking, especially when the wind whips off the North Sea.

Fairmont St Andrews - Torrance
The Torrance Course, the Kittocks' sister at the Fairmont resort, was originally designed by the late, great Ryder Cup legend Sam Torrance. It was later extensively remodeled by Gary Stephenson to give it a more traditional Scottish links character. Torrance's vision was to create a walking-friendly course that felt like a classic links despite its clifftop location. This was achieved by adding revetted bunkering and undulating fairways that encourage the "ground game" so vital to Scottish golf.

The Torrance has a high-profile tournament pedigree, having served as a Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship and hosting the DP World Tour's Hero Open. It is a challenging par-72 that demands strategic thinking; Sam Torrance himself was known as a master tactician, and his course reflects that, with subtle risks and rewards built into every hole. With the iconic Fairmont hotel looming in the background and the sea stretching out to the horizon, it offers a luxury golfing experience that blends modern amenities with the timeless challenge of coastal golf.

Falkirk
Known locally as "Carmuirs" Falkirk Golf Club is one of the premier parkland venues in Central Scotland. Founded in 1922, the course was laid out by the prolific James Braid, whose influence is evident in the clever use of the natural landscape and the strategic bunkering that protects the greens. The course is characterized by its lush, mature woodland and remarkably consistent playing surfaces. It serves as a green oasis in an area otherwise known for its industrial heritage, providing a peaceful and highly scenic round of golf.

The club has long been a hub for competitive amateur golf, frequently hosting the Stirlingshire County Championships. Its layout is famously fair; there are few "tricky" holes, but the length and the tightness of the tree-lined fairways require a disciplined approach. The signature 14th hole, a par-three across a valley, is often cited as the most beautiful on the course. Famous for its friendly membership and an active social scene, Falkirk remains a traditional Scottish club that prides itself on maintaining Braid's original design principles while providing a modern, high-quality experience for its members and visitors.

Falkirk - Antonine
Located on the outskirts of the village of Bonnybridge, the Antonine course (part of the Falkirk Golf Club ecosystem but distinct in character) is steeped in ancient history.
