Located just inland from the famous Bogside links, Irvine Ravenspark is a high-quality municipal course that offers a fascinating hybrid of links and parkland golf. Opened in 1907 to provide accessible golf to the local community, the course was refined by the esteemed architect J.R. Stutt. It features the same sandy, free-draining soil as its illustrious neighbor, ensuring excellent playing conditions year-round. While the front nine has a more open, links-like feel, the back nine moves into a more wooded environment with several challenging water hazards.

Ravenspark has a strong reputation for producing high-caliber amateur talent, including several Scottish internationalists. The course has frequently hosted regional qualifying for the Open Championship and the Scottish Public Courses Championship. One of the highlights of the round is the stretch of holes that run alongside the River Irvine, requiring precise club selection and a steady nerve. It is a friendly, inclusive club that prides itself on offering a championship-standard test at a public price point. For many local golfers, Ravenspark is the "people's favorite" providing a varied and engaging round of golf in the heart of Ayrshire.

Isle of Gigha
The Isle of Gigha Golf Club offers one of the most unique and remote golfing experiences in the world. This 9-hole course is situated on a community-owned island off the Kintyre peninsula. Established in the early 20th century, the course is a "natural links" in the truest sense, with the routing dictated by the rugged shoreline and the ancient rocky outcrops of the island. The views from the course—stretching across the Atlantic to the Isles of Islay and Jura—are simply breathtaking and often distract golfers from their game.

The course is famous for its lack of formality; there are no tee times, and the "honesty box" for green fees reflects the island's relaxed and trusting atmosphere. The layout is quirky and challenging, featuring blind shots and small greens that are perched on natural plateaus. It is a place where you can find total peace, with the only sounds being the waves and the wind. For the "golfing adventurer" a trip to Gigha is a mandatory pilgrimage to play a course that has remained unchanged by modern design, offering a pure and atmospheric connection to the origins of the game.

Isle of Eriska (9-Hole)
The Isle of Eriska offers what is arguably the most exclusive and picturesque short-course experience in the British Isles. Located on a private 300-acre island off the west coast near Oban, the 9-hole course is part of the world-renowned Isle of Eriska Hotel and Spa. The course was designed by Howard Swan and is intended to provide a luxury recreational experience for guests of the estate. It is a "hidden" course in the most literal sense, accessible only by a private bridge that connects the island to the mainland.

The holes wind through ancient woodland and along the rugged shoreline, offering views of Loch Linnhe and the Morvern mountains that are nothing short of cinematic. While it is a shorter course, the island's terrain provides natural defenses, with rocky outcrops and the sea breeze adding to the difficulty. It is a favorite haunt of celebrities and high-profile guests seeking total privacy. Playing here is less about the scorecard and more about the atmosphere—the scent of wild garlic in the air, the sight of golden eagles overhead, and the absolute silence that comes with playing on your own private island.

Isle of Harris
The Isle of Harris Golf Club, situated at Scarista, is often cited as the most beautiful 9-hole course in the world. Originally laid out in 1912 by Alick Marling, the course famously overlooks the turquoise waters of the Atlantic and the white sands of the Hebrides. It lay dormant for decades after the war until a group of determined locals revived it in 1985. The "Links of Harris" is a raw, natural experience where the fairways are grazed by sheep and the hazards are dictated by the rugged shoreline.


The club gained global fame when Sir Nick Faldo played here in the early 1990s as a quiet retreat to sharpen his game for the Masters. He was so charmed by the place that he left a signed £5 note in the "honesty box" for his green fee; that same fiver is now framed in the clubhouse and serves as the trophy for the annual "Faldo Fiver" competition. Without a single bunker to speak of, the course relies on the wind and the punishing Atlantic surf to defend its par. It is a spiritual home for golfers who prefer the elements and the "honesty" of the game over manicured perfection.

Isle of Seil
Located just over the famous "Bridge Over the Atlantic" the Isle of Seil Golf Club is a quirky and charming 9-hole course that defines community-led golf. Formed in 1995 after residents realized the island's potential for a local track, it was built primarily through the manual labor of its members. The course occupies the site of a 1910 layout that had been lost to time. It is a parkland-style course set on a working farm, meaning players must often negotiate the local livestock alongside the undulating fairways.

While it does not have a "celebrity" architect, the routing was cleverly devised by the founding committee to maximize the views of the Slate Islands. The club is a pioneer in modern golf governance, having been one of the first in Scotland to consistently elect female captains based purely on merit. Its most notable tournament is the Highland Arts Burns Crystal, an 18-hole open that draws players from across Argyll. It is a "social" club in the best sense, where the 19th hole is often as important as the first, making it a favorite for those touring the Inner Hebrides.

Isle of Skye
The Isle of Skye Golf Club, originally known as Sconser, sits in the dramatic shadow of the Cuillin Mountains. The course was designed in 1964 by the esteemed Dr. Frank Deighton, a former Walker Cup player and one of Scotland's most talented amateur golfers. Deighton's layout is a masterful use of a narrow strip of coastal land, offering a 9-hole experience with eighteen different tee positions that completely change the angle of attack for the second loop.


The club has a rugged history; for years, electric fences were required around the greens to keep the local sheep at bay. In 1987, the club rebranded to its current name to better reflect its status as the island's premier golfing destination. Famous for its "Sea Hole" where a wayward slice will see your ball vanish into the Raasay Sound, the course is a visual masterpiece. Deighton was so fond of his creation that his ashes were scattered on the fairways following his passing in 2018. It is a course that requires a low ball flight and a steady nerve, particularly when the Highland mist rolls off the peaks.


Jedburgh
Jedburgh Golf Club is a scenic parkland course that embodies the quiet dignity of the Scottish Borders. Founded in 1893, the course was significantly improved in the early 20th century with input from the legendary Willie Park Jr., a two-time Open Champion. Park's influence is seen in the clever use of the natural slopes of the Dunion hill, ensuring that very few lies are ever truly flat. The course is famously divided by the Jed Water, which provides a serene soundtrack to a round that is often quite taxing on the legs.

The club has long been a pillar of the Borders golfing community, producing numerous regional champions. It is known for its "Lothian" greens—slick, undulating surfaces that require a soft touch. While it may not host the massive professional events of the coast, it is a regular venue for the Borders Golfers' Association championships. The clubhouse, overlooking the historic abbey town, offers a warm, traditional welcome. For those who enjoy the "mountain goat" style of golf where elevation change is the primary hazard, Jedburgh is a classic example of late-Victorian inland design.

Keith
Keith Golf Club in Banffshire has a history of resilience. The original 1901 course was lost to the "Dig for Victory" campaign during World War II, but the club was resurrected in the 1960s thanks to a generous land donation from Colonel G.B. Kynoch. The current 18-hole layout was designed by Roy Phimister, the professional at Nairn, who created a parkland course that feels significantly more mature than its years. It is characterized by the Drum Burn, which snakes through the property and creates several "do or die" approach shots.

The club is a focal point for the local distilling community, with many members hailing from the world-famous Strathisla and Glenkeith distilleries. The layout is relatively short but notoriously tight, with gorse and broom punishing any lack of discipline off the tee. One of its most famous features is the 6th hole, which requires a blind shot over a corner of "Out of Bounds" territory to reach a green perched on a steep bank. It is an honest, welcoming Highland club that rewards accuracy and strategic play over raw distance, making it a favorite for local society outings.

Kelso
Kelso Golf Club offers one of the most unique settings in Scotland, as much of the course is situated within the perimeter of the Kelso Racecourse. Established in 1893, the course was significantly refined by James Braid, who managed to weave a challenging 18-hole parkland layout around the jumps and the grandstands. This proximity to the "sport of kings" gives the course a grand, expansive feel, with the flat racecourse land providing a fast-playing surface that stays remarkably dry even in the winter.

The club has a prestigious reputation in the Borders and has hosted the Scottish Seniors' Championship. Because the course is largely flat, it is an easy walk, but Braid's signature "crater" bunkers and the occasional racecourse rail provide plenty of strategic obstacles. Famous visitors over the years have included many of the UK's leading jockeys and trainers, who often squeeze in a round between races. It is a course of two halves: the holes inside the track are wide and inviting, while those outside the rails are tighter and more technical. Kelso is a masterclass in sharing space, offering a refined and historic golfing experience.

Kemnay
Kemnay Golf Club is a lush, expansive parkland course in Aberdeenshire that has evolved significantly since its founding in 1908. While it began as a modest 9-hole track, it was transformed into a premier 18-hole venue in the 1990s under the guidance of architect Graeme Webster. Webster's design philosophy—"playability for all"—is evident here, with generous fairways that allow for a fun round while still incorporating enough bunkers and water to challenge the low handicapper.

The course is famous for its "Amen Corner" stretch near the end of the round, where the 16th, 17th, and 18th holes can ruin a good scorecard. The 18th in particular, a par-four with a daunting approach over a pond to a green in front of the clubhouse, was a later addition by Webster that provided the course with a truly dramatic finish. Kemnay is a frequent host of the Aberdeenshire County Championships and is widely regarded as having some of the most consistent greens in the North East. It is a friendly, modern club that manages to feel like a private estate, offering a high-quality, polished parkland experience.

Kilbirnie Place
Kilbirnie Place Golf Club, situated in the hills of North Ayrshire, is a course built on industrial pride. Founded in 1923, the course was established largely by and for the workers of the local steelworks. Originally a 9-hole course on the site of an old estate, it was extended to 18 holes by the members themselves, who hand-cleared the land to ensure the club's survival. This "DIY" history has resulted in a course with a rugged, honest character that follows the natural, rolling contours of the Garnock Valley.

The course is known for its spectacular views toward the Firth of Clyde and the mountains of Arran. While it lacks a "famous name" architect, the layout is highly respected for its natural defense; the "Place" burn comes into play on several holes, requiring precise iron play. The club maintains a fiercely loyal membership and a reputation for being one of the most welcoming in the West of Scotland. It is the perfect example of a "working man's" course that has matured into a scenic and testing parkland gem, proving that great golf is often born from the passion of its players.

Killermont
Killermont is the historic home of the Glasgow Golf Club, the 9th oldest in the world. The course was laid out in 1903 by the legendary Old Tom Morris and was famously his final full course design before his passing. Set within the grounds of a magnificent B-listed Georgian mansion that serves as the clubhouse, Killermont is a sanctuary of traditionalism. It is a pure parkland experience, characterized by massive, ancient trees and the winding River Kelvin, which guards several of the greens.


The club has hosted the Scottish PGA Championship and the Scottish Amateur, and its membership roll is a "who's who" of Glasgow's professional history. Unlike the club's other course at Gailes, which is a links, Killermont is a tactical, refined test that emphasizes placement over power. One of its most daunting features is the 2nd hole, a par-three of over 240 yards that remains one of the toughest one-shotters in the country. To play Killermont is to walk in the footsteps of the "Grand Old Man of Golf" on a canvas that has remained largely untouched for over a century.

Killin
Killin Golf Club is widely considered one of the most scenic 9-hole courses in the British Isles. Established in 1911 and situated at the heart of the Breadalbane region, the course was laid out with advice from John Duncan, the professional at Stirling. The course is a Highland parkland masterpiece, featuring dramatic elevation changes and views of the Falls of Dochart and the surrounding peaks of Ben Lawers. It is a course that doesn't just ask you to play golf; it asks you to admire the landscape.

The club has a rich community history, having been founded by a diverse group that included the local schoolmaster, the gamekeeper, and the minister. The signature hole is the par-three 9th, "The Chasm" where players must hit from an elevated tee over a deep, wooded ravine to a green perched on the hillside. While it hasn't hosted international professional tours, it is a favorite for visitors and was famously a haunt of the novelist Sir Walter Scott's descendants. It is an atmospheric, rugged, and intensely beautiful course that embodies the wild heart of Perthshire golf.

Kilmacolm
Kilmacolm Golf Club is a stunning moorland course that feels like a Highland escape just half an hour from Glasgow. Established in 1891, the layout was refined by the prolific James Braid, who capitalized on the rugged, undulating terrain of the Knapps. The course is a riot of color in the summer, with purple heather and yellow gorse framing the fairways. Because it sits on high ground, it offers expansive views across the Clyde Valley to the peaks of Ben Lomond and the southern Highlands.

The club has a prestigious history and has produced many fine players, including the 1935 Scottish Amateur champion Jack McLean. The course is notoriously difficult to play to your handicap, as the springy turf and natural hollows create unpredictable lies. One of its most famous features is the "Valley of the Shadow" a deep natural ravine that must be negotiated on the back nine. It is a traditional, friendly club that prides itself on maintaining Braid's strategic vision, offering a "pure" golfing experience where the wind and the terrain are the primary defenders.

Kilmarnock Barassie
Kilmarnock (Barassie) Golf Club is a heavyweight of Scottish links golf. Founded in 1887, the course underwent a significant redesign in the 1930s by James Braid, creating a layout that is often mentioned in the same breath as Troon and Prestwick. It is a classic links, characterized by deep bunkers, firm fairways, and a constant, shifting sea breeze. The club has an immense tournament pedigree, having served as a Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship and hosting the Scottish Amateur on multiple occasions.


Famous members have included several Walker Cup players, and the club's professional tradition is strong. The course is unique for having 27 holes, with the main 18-hole championship layout known for its brutal closing stretch. The railway line runs parallel to several holes, adding to the traditional Ayrshire links atmosphere. Barassie is a true ball-striker's course; it doesn't rely on gimmicks, only on the quality of its turf and the strategic placement of its hazards. For any golfer touring the Ayrshire coast, it is an essential and historically significant stop.

Kilmarnock Barassie - Hillhouse
The Hillhouse course at Barassie consists of nine holes that, when combined with the nine holes of the "new" loop, create a second full 18-hole experience at this prestigious club. Despite being the "secondary" layout, Hillhouse is maintained to the same exacting championship standards as the main links. It offers a slightly different character, blending traditional links turf with areas that feel more like a sheltered heathland course, providing a varied and engaging round for those who have already tackled the main championship track.

Hillhouse is particularly popular with members for a quick, competitive game and serves as an excellent venue for junior development. It shares the same sandy, free-draining soil that makes Barassie playable throughout the year. While it may lack the international spotlight of the Open Qualifying holes, it features several tricky par-threes and tight fairways that demand precision. It is the perfect example of the "depth of quality" found at Scotland's premier golf clubs, ensuring that every golfer—from the high handicapper to the elite amateur—has a world-class surface to play on.

Kilspindie
Kilspindie Golf Club is a "hidden gem" in the truest sense of the word. Tucked away on the Aberlady Bay in East Lothian, this 18th-oldest club in the world offers a pure, traditional links experience that is almost entirely unchanged since the 19th century. Originally a 9-hole course, it was extended to 18 in 1898 by the local professional Ben Sayers and Willie Park Jr. It is a short, par-69 course, but what it lacks in length, it makes up for in sheer charm and coastal beauty.

The course is famous for its intimacy; the first hole starts right beside the clubhouse wall, and the sea is in view on almost every shot. Notable members have included legends like Babe Zaharias, who reportedly loved the course for its fun and accessibility. Kilspindie is the perfect antidote to the "modern championship" course; it is a walking-friendly links where a creative short game is far more valuable than a 300-yard drive. With its hidden bunkers, stone walls, and proximity to the bird sanctuary, it is one of the most atmospheric and enjoyable rounds of golf in the "Cradle of Golf."

Kilsyth Lennox
Kilsyth Lennox Golf Club is a rugged and scenic parkland-moorland hybrid that sits at the foot of the Kilsyth Hills. Founded in 1905, the course was redesigned and extended to its current 18-hole form in the 1970s. The layout is characterized by significant elevation changes, offering spectacular views across the Kelvin Valley toward the city of Glasgow and beyond. The course is built on a series of natural terraces, meaning you will often find yourself hitting from elevated tees to greens tucked into the folds of the hills.

While it hasn't hosted the major professional tours, Kilsyth Lennox has a reputation for producing tough, resilient golfers who excel in the Stirlingshire county circuits. The course is known for its "Burn holes" where the local water system creates natural hazards that require careful management. It is a traditional, community-focused club that offers a warm welcome to visitors. The 13th hole, "The Spectacles" is a highlight, requiring a blind shot over twin mounds to reach the green. It is a course that rewards local knowledge and a steady nerve, offering a high-value, authentic Scottish golfing experience.

Kingarrock Hickory
Kingarrock Hickory Golf is a unique time-capsule and the only course of its kind in Europe. Located at Hill of Tarvit Mansion in Fife, this 9-hole course is played exclusively with original wooden-shafted hickory clubs and authentic low-compression balls from the 1920s. The course was originally the private plaything of the Sharp family, who owned the estate, but it was lost to farming during the war and only painstakingly restored in 2008 using the original 1924 plans.

There are no modern amenities here—no yardage markers, no graphite shafts, and no oversized heads. Golfers are provided with a heritage bag containing five clubs: a brassie, a mashie, a niblick, a mid-iron, and a putter. Playing Kingarrock is a masterclass in the "art" of the game over the "science." Notable visitors have included golf historians from around the world seeking to experience the game as Bobby Jones or Walter Hagen would have played it. It is a peaceful, nostalgic experience that reminds every golfer that the heart of the game is about feel, creativity, and a touch of luck.

Kinghorn
Kinghorn Golf Club is a delightful seaside links that offers one of the most enjoyable "walks with a view" in Fife. Founded in 1887, the course was originally designed by the legendary Old Tom Morris. It is a short but tricky layout that clings to the cliffs and hillsides overlooking the Firth of Forth. Because of its elevation, the wind is a constant and formidable opponent, often turning a modest par-four into a grueling struggle for par.

The course is municipal in nature, meaning it remains a vital part of the local community. While it lacks the length of championship tracks, it features some of the most memorable holes in the region, including several that require shots across deep coastal ravines. The view toward Edinburgh and the Bass Rock is a constant companion throughout the round. Kinghorn is proof that Old Tom Morris could create a challenging and engaging layout on any piece of land, regardless of size. It is a friendly, accessible course that provides a high-octane links experience without the high-octane green fee.

King James VI
King James VI Golf Club holds the distinction of being the only golf course in the world situated on a river island. Located on Moncreiffe Island in the middle of the River Tay in Perth, the club was founded in 1858 and the course was designed by Old Tom Morris in 1897. Access to the course is via a narrow walkway attached to a railway bridge, which immediately sets the tone for a unique golfing adventure. The island setting means the course is perfectly flat, but it is surrounded by the rushing waters of the Tay and lined with mature trees.

The club is steeped in history and was named after the Scottish monarch who was a known enthusiast of the game. Because it is an island, the course has a unique microclimate and is famously lush and green throughout the summer. It has hosted many regional championships and is a favorite for those seeking a tranquil, "secret" golfing experience right in the heart of the city. While the river rarely comes directly into play, the psychological pressure of playing on an island—and the occasional sound of the trains overhead—adds a fascinating layer of character to this historic Old Tom Morris gem.

Kings (Inverness)
Kings Golf Club in Inverness (formerly Torvean) is a relatively new addition to the Highland golf scene but one that has made a massive impact. Opened in 2019 to replace the old municipal course, the layout was designed by Stuart Rennie. It is a modern, championship-standard parkland course that was built to rival the quality of the famous links nearby. Rennie utilized the rolling terrain to create a "stadium" feel, with large, undulating greens and expansive white sand bunkers that give it a very high-end, professional look.

Despite its young age, Kings has already hosted several prestigious amateur events and is a regular venue for the PGA Highlands Open. The course offers spectacular views over the city of Inverness and the Caledonian Canal. The signature hole is the par-three 14th, which plays across a valley to a well-guarded green. The modern clubhouse is a hub of activity and reflects the club's ambition to be a premier golfing destination. It is the perfect example of how modern design can create a challenging, visually stunning course that complements the traditional Highland landscape.

Kings Acre
Located on the southern outskirts of Edinburgh, Kings Acre is a modern parkland course and academy that has become a center for golf development in the Lothians. Opened in the late 1990s, the course was designed by Graeme Webster and features a layout that is both challenging for the low-handicapper and accessible for the casual player. The design is characterized by large, USGA-spec greens and several significant water hazards that require strategic navigation.

Kings Acre is perhaps best known for its world-class academy and practice facilities, which have attracted many of Scotland's leading professionals for practice sessions. The course itself is beautifully manicured and offers views toward the Pentland Hills. It has a "commercial" feel in the best sense—professional, well-organized, and focused on providing a high-quality experience. The par-five 18th is a fantastic finishing hole, requiring a brave approach over water to reach the green in two. It is a popular choice for corporate days and for golfers in the capital who want a polished, modern round of golf without the formality of an ancient private club.

Kingsbarns
Kingsbarns Golf Links is a modern masterpiece that feels as though it has been part of the Fife coastline for centuries. While golf was played here in the 1700s, the current course was designed by Kyle Phillips and opened in 2000. It is a man-made links of extraordinary scale, featuring wide, undulating fairways and tiered greens that offer spectacular views of the North Sea from every single hole. It is most famous as a co-host of the Alfred Dunhill Links Championship on the DP World Tour, where it consistently ranks as a favorite among the world's top professionals.


The course has hosted many of the game's greats, including Tiger Woods and Rory McIlroy. Its signature hole, the par-three 15th, requires a nerve-wracking carry over the surging Atlantic waves to a green perched on a rocky outcrop. Unlike the traditional "out and back" links, Kingsbarns is designed in two loops that maximize the dramatic elevation changes of the site. It is a high-end, world-class experience that combines the rugged beauty of traditional links golf with the polished service of a premier resort.

Kingsfield
Kingsfield Golf & Leisure, located near Linlithgow, is a vibrant and modern facility designed to foster a love for the game. The 9-hole course was designed by Graeme Webster and serves as a high-quality "short course" that challenges experienced players while remaining accessible to beginners. The greens are built to USGA specifications, ensuring that the putting surfaces are among the truest in West Lothian. It is a parkland layout that emphasizes the "fun" aspect of golf, featuring wide fairways and clever bunkering.


The facility is particularly famous for its "Ochil Course" putting green, a massive, undulating 18-hole putting surface inspired by the Himalayas at St Andrews. Kingsfield is also home to a top-tier academy where several Scottish professionals, including Stephen Gallacher, have been known to practice. It is a community-focused hub that includes a TaylorMade performance center and high-tech driving range. For those looking for a relaxed yet high-quality golfing experience that fits into a busy schedule, Kingsfield is a pioneer of the modern Scottish golf model.


Kingsknowe
Kingsknowe Golf Club is a classic Edinburgh parkland course with a history dating back to 1908. The layout was originally designed by the legendary Alec Herd, the 1902 Open Champion, and later refined by the "busiest man in golf" James Braid. The course is characterized by its hilly terrain and mature woodland, providing a peaceful escape from the nearby city center. Its most distinctive feature is the railway line that runs parallel to several holes, adding a touch of Victorian industrial charm to the lush surroundings.

The club has a proud history of competitive amateur golf and has hosted numerous Lothians County events. Notable members have included prominent figures in Edinburgh's legal and medical professions, reflecting the club's traditional status. The greens at Kingsknowe are notoriously tricky, often sloping significantly and requiring a precise read. The signature 12th hole offers a stunning view across the city toward the Pentland Hills. It is a friendly, well-established club that rewards accuracy and stamina, offering a quintessential "city parkland" round with a historic pedigree.

Kings Links
Kings Links is a historic coastal course that provides the city of Aberdeen with a pure links experience right on its doorstep. As one of the oldest sites of golf in the world—with records of the game being played here as far back as 1625—it is a place of immense heritage. The current 18-hole layout is a traditional "out and back" links, running parallel to the North Sea beach. It is a flat, walking-friendly course characterized by firm turf, hidden pot bunkers, and the ever-present, biting coastal wind.

The course is municipal in nature, meaning it has served generations of Aberdonians as a training ground for the more famous links nearby. While it may not host the professional tours, its defense lies in its exposure to the elements; on a calm day, it is a delight, but in a gale, it is a formidable test of low-ball flight. It is famously free-draining, making it a popular choice for year-round golf. For visitors, Kings Links offers an authentic, "no-frills" connection to the origins of the game, played on land that has seen over 400 years of golfing history.

Kingussie
Founded in 1891, Kingussie Golf Club is a spectacular Highland moorland course that sits high above the Spey Valley. The layout was refined by both Old Tom Morris and Harry Vardon, two of the most iconic figures in the history of the sport. Their influence is evident in the way the course works harmoniously with the natural peat hags, heather, and birch woodland. It is a physically demanding course, but the reward is a series of panoramic views of the Cairngorm Mountains that are among the best in Scotland.

The club is known for its warm, traditional Highland hospitality and its small, tricky greens. Kingussie has long been a favorite for visiting societies who appreciate the "raw" nature of the terrain. The Gynack Burn meanders through the course, providing a natural hazard on several holes. It is a course that requires creativity rather than brute force, as the undulating fairways and mountain breezes demand a variety of shot shapes. To play Kingussie is to experience the "Golden Age" of golf architecture in one of the most breathtaking natural settings in the country.

Kinross - Montgomery
The Montgomery Course at the Green Hotel in Kinross is a premier parkland test that offers a "championship" feel. Designed by Colton & Co. and opened in the early 20th century, it was significantly updated by Donald Steel to meet modern standards. The course is named after the local estate and is characterized by its large, undulating greens and significant water hazards. It has a reputations for being the more challenging of the two courses at the resort, often used for regional qualifying and high-level amateur tournaments.

The layout is lush and well-wooded, providing a sheltered environment that contrasts with the windswept links of the coast. Famous visitors over the decades have included many of Scotland's leading political and business figures who frequent the hotel. The signature par-three 16th, played over a pond to a well-guarded green, is a notorious "card-wrecker." The Montgomery offers a polished, professional golfing experience with manicured fairways and fast, true greens, making it a staple for those touring the heart of Scotland.

Kinross - Bruce
The Bruce Course, the Montgomery's sibling at the Green Hotel, is often perceived as the more relaxed and "fun" of the two Kinross layouts, but it is certainly no pushover. Named after the legendary Scottish King, the course features a unique par-73 layout that includes four par-fives on the back nine. This makes it a favorite for long hitters and those looking to improve their birdie count. The design is more open than the Montgomery, allowing for a bit more freedom off the tee while still requiring a sharp short game.

The Bruce is particularly popular for society outings and corporate days due to its playability and scenic views over Loch Leven. Despite its more "user-friendly" reputation, it features several tricky dog-legs and well-placed bunkers that require strategic thinking. The course shares the same high maintenance standards as its sister, ensuring a quality playing surface year-round. It is a delightful, walking-friendly parkland course that provides a perfect complement to the tougher Montgomery, making the Kinross resort a comprehensive destination for any golfing group.

Kintore
Kintore Golf Club, located in Aberdeenshire, is a fascinating hybrid course that has evolved from a 9-hole layout founded in 1911 into a premier 18-hole parkland venue. The "original" nine holes are characterized by their hilly, moorland nature, while the newer nine, added in the 1990s and designed by Graeme Webster, offer a more modern, flatter parkland experience. This contrast makes for a varied and engaging round, as the player must adapt their strategy as they move between the two distinct sections of the course.

The club is a hub of the local community and is known for its friendly, inclusive atmosphere. The views from the higher holes stretch across the Don Valley toward the peak of Bennachie, providing a classic Highland backdrop. Kintore has hosted various North East District championships and is highly regarded for the quality of its greens. The signature 13th hole, "The Tuach" is a challenging par-four that requires a precise drive to a narrowing fairway. It is a course that perfectly blends the old and the new, offering a high-value and scenic round of golf in the heart of the "Garioch."

Kirkcaldy
Kirkcaldy Golf Club, known locally as "Balwearie" is a magnificent parkland course that was famously designed by Tom Morris Jr. (Young Tom Morris) in 1904. To play a course designed by the four-time Open Champion is a rare privilege, and the layout still retains much of its original character. The course is set in a secluded valley and is characterized by its lush, mature woodland and the Raith Burn, which meanders through the property and comes into play on several of the most challenging holes.

The club has a prestigious history and has produced many fine amateur players who have represented Fife and Scotland. It is a regular host of the Fife County Championships. The layout is known for its fast, sloping greens and its "stadium" finish in front of the clubhouse. While it is located near the town center, the dense tree-lining provides a sense of total isolation. Kirkcaldy is a "pure" golf club that prides itself on its traditions and its challenging, high-quality playing surface. It is a must-play for anyone interested in the history of the Morris family and the development of parkland golf in Fife.

Kirkcudbright
Established in 1893, Kirkcudbright Golf Club (pronounced "Kir-coo-bree") is a beautiful parkland course that offers spectacular views over the Dee Estuary and the historic "Artists' Town." The course was originally a 9-hole layout but was extended to 18 holes in the early 20th century with input from the legendary James Braid. Braid's influence is evident in the strategic use of the naturally undulating terrain and the well-protected greens. The course is famously lush, featuring a wide variety of mature trees and vibrant gorse.

The club has long been a favorite of the artistic community that made the town famous, and the clubhouse features several works by local artists. Notable tournaments held here include the South of Scotland Championship. The layout is relatively compact, requiring accuracy and a delicate touch around the greens rather than brute force. The 13th hole, "The Pulpit" offers a panoramic view that is often cited as the best in Dumfries and Galloway. It is a friendly, scenic, and historic club that provides a tranquil and refined golfing experience in one of the most beautiful corners of the country.

Kirkhill
Kirkhill Golf Club, located on the southern fringes of Glasgow, is a prestigious parkland course that celebrated its centenary in 2010. The layout was designed by the legendary James Braid, who masterfully utilized the rolling terrain to create a course that tests every facet of a player's game. It is characterized by its lush, tree-lined fairways and exceptionally fast, undulating greens. Because of its elevated position, the course offers panoramic views across the Clyde Valley toward the Campsie Fells.


The club has a storied history of competitive success, with its members frequently featuring in the final stages of the Lanarkshire County Championships. Notable members have included several professional footballers and prominent local businessmen, contributing to a vibrant and social atmosphere. The signature 13th hole, a par-three known as "The Dell" requires a precise shot to a green nestled in a natural hollow. Kirkhill remains a "pure" golf club that prioritizes the quality of its playing surface, offering a challenging and traditional Braid experience just a stone's throw from the city.

Kirkintilloch
Established in 1895, Kirkintilloch Golf Club is a beautiful parkland gem situated at the foot of the Campsie Fells. The course reached its modern 18-hole form in the 1920s under the guidance of James Braid. Braid's signature strategic bunkering is evident throughout, forcing golfers to think their way around the course rather than simply relying on distance. The layout is divided by the Antonine Wall, providing a unique historical backdrop to several holes.

The club is famous for its "Amen Corner" stretch near the end of the round, where water hazards and tight fairways can quickly derail a good score. It has hosted various Dunbartonshire County events and is a regular venue for the PGA Scotland regional circuit. The clubhouse is known for its warm hospitality and overlooks a fantastic finishing hole that requires an approach over a burn. It is a traditional, friendly club that offers a varied and engaging test of golf, perfectly blending the tranquility of the countryside with a historic architectural pedigree.

Kirriemuir
Kirriemuir Golf Club, located in the "Gateway to the Glens" is a scenic parkland course with a design history involving the great James Braid. Established in 1908, the course sits on the edge of the Angus glens, offering views of the Grampian Mountains that are nothing short of spectacular. The layout is characterized by its wide fairways and a diverse range of mature trees, including silver birch and pine. It is the birthplace of Sir J.M. Barrie, the creator of Peter Pan, and the club embraces this heritage with several holes named in honor of the "Boy Who Never Grew Up."

The course is known for its immaculate conditioning and its remarkably true greens. While it is not an overly long course, the subtle undulations and the local breezes provide a constant challenge. Notable tournaments held here include the Scottish Seniors' Championship. It is a welcoming, community-focused club where the focus is on the sheer enjoyment of the game in a beautiful Highland setting. For those looking for a tranquil and historic round of golf in the heart of Angus, Kirriemuir offers a refined and charming experience.

Knightswood
Knightswood Golf Course is one of Glasgow's most popular municipal 9-hole layouts, providing an essential gateway to the game for the city's residents. Originally part of a larger estate, the course was opened to the public in the mid-20th century to meet the rising demand for accessible sport. It is a flat, easy-to-walk parkland course that features surprisingly large greens and well-placed bunkering. Despite its urban location, the course is remarkably peaceful, acting as a green lung for the West End of Glasgow.

The course is a vital "nursery" for the local golfing community, where many of Glasgow's club golfers first learned their swing. While it does not host professional tours, it is a hub for the Glasgow Municipal Golf Clubs' Association and hosts regular amateur competitions. The layout is designed to be forgiving for beginners while still providing enough of a challenge to keep experienced players coming back for a quick afternoon round. It is a shining example of public-sector golf, proving that high-quality, fun, and affordable golf can thrive in the heart of a major city.

Turnberry - King Robert the Bruce
The King Robert the Bruce course at the world-famous Turnberry resort is a spectacular links that underwent a total transformation in 2017. Designed by Martin Ebert (who also handled the Ailsa redesign), it was named in honor of the Scottish King who was born in the adjacent Turnberry Castle in 1274. The course utilizes the dramatic, rugged coastline of Ayrshire, offering views of the Ailsa Craig and the lighthouse that rival those of its world-ranking sibling.

While it shares the same hallowed turf as the Ailsa, the King Robert the Bruce course has its own distinct personality, with several holes playing across high ridges and deep coastal ravines. It has hosted the AIG Women's Open and remains a favorite for high-end corporate events. Famous visitors include every major name in modern golf, from Tiger Woods to Jack Nicklaus. It is an uncompromising and visually stunning test of links golf, featuring "infinity" greens and deep, revetted bunkers that demand total commitment from the tee to the cup.

Kyles of Bute
The Kyles of Bute Golf Club, situated near Tighnabruaich, is a 9-hole moorland course that offers what many consider to be the most breathtaking views in the British Isles. Established in 1906, the course is perched on the hills overlooking the "Kyles"—the narrow straits of water separating the Isle of Bute from the Cowal Peninsula. The layout is rugged and natural, with the fairways carved out of the heather and gorse.

The club is an "honesty box" facility, reflecting the relaxed and welcoming nature of the local community. While it lacks a celebrity architect, the routing is genius, forcing golfers to navigate steep hills and rocky outcrops. The most famous feature is the 4th hole, where the view of the Loch Ruel is so distracting it's a wonder anyone hits the fairway. It is a place of total tranquility, where the only sound is the wind and the cry of the gulls. For the golfing adventurer, a round at Kyles of Bute is a mandatory pilgrimage to see the raw, wild beauty of Argyll from the fairways.

Ladybank
Ladybank Golf Club in Fife is one of the most prestigious and historic heathland courses in the world. Originally a 6-hole course designed by Old Tom Morris in 1879, it was eventually expanded to 18 holes, with the layout we see today largely being the work of J.R. Stutt. Ladybank is a premier Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship and has hosted some of the greatest names in the game during their quest to reach the world's oldest major.

The course is a sanctuary of Scots pine, heather, and silver birch. Famous members include Dale Reid, the legendary Solheim Cup captain, and the club has a reputation for producing elite amateur talent. Unlike the nearby links, Ladybank offers a sheltered, tactical test where a driver is often best left in the bag in favor of a well-placed iron shot. The greens are famously fast and true, requiring a delicate touch. It is a refined, aristocratic club that offers a pure and uncompromising test of golf, representing the very pinnacle of Scottish inland course design.

Lamlash
Situated on the beautiful Isle of Arran, Lamlash Golf Club is an 18-hole course that offers a dramatic "Highland in miniature" experience. Founded in 1889, the course was redesigned by the 1883 Open Champion Willie Fernie, who utilized the steep, undulating terrain to create a layout that provides constant challenges and rewards. The views across Lamlash Bay to the Holy Isle are cinematic, particularly from the higher holes on the back nine.

The club is a focal point of the island's community and has a history of hosting the Arran Open Championship. Notable visitors have included world leaders and celebrities seeking the privacy of the island. The course is known for its short but tricky par-threes and its lush, springy turf. The signature hole, the par-three 4th, requires a shot over a valley to a green perched on a plateau. It is a friendly, traditional club where the hospitality is as memorable as the views. For anyone visiting Arran, Lamlash provides a rugged and authentic Scottish golfing experience that tests your skill and your fitness.

Lanark
Lanark Golf Club is the 25th oldest golf club in the world and a true heavyweight of the Scottish moorland game. Founded in 1851, the course was originally laid out by Old Tom Morris and later refined by James Braid. It sits on high ground overlooking the Clyde Valley and is characterized by its firm, sandy, and fast-draining soil—giving it the playing characteristics of a links course despite being miles from the sea. It is a perennial Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship.

The course is a masterclass in strategic design, with deep bunkers and thick heather punishing any wayward shots. Famous members have included titans of the Scottish textile industry and champion amateurs like Charlie Green. Lanark is known for its impeccable conditioning and its sense of history; the clubhouse is a treasure trove of early golfing artifacts. The layout is a stern but fair test that requires every club in the bag and a creative approach to shot-making. It is one of the most respected courses in the country, offering a world-class experience in a peaceful and historic setting.

Langholm
Langholm Golf Club is a charming and historic 9-hole course situated in the "Muckle Toon" of the Scottish Borders. Established in 1892, the course is built on the steep slopes of the Castle Hill, offering views that stretch across the Esk Valley toward the Solway Firth. The layout was refined by the 1883 Open Champion Willie Fernie, who used the natural elevation changes to create a course that plays much longer than its yardage.

The club is famous for its connection to the Langholm Common Riding, one of the oldest and grandest border festivals, with several holes crossing the ancient boundaries of the town. It is a "mountain goat" course that requires a good level of fitness, but the exhilaration of the elevated tees is unparalleled. Notable members have included local sporting legends and prominent members of the Borders wool industry. It is a traditional, unpretentious club that prides itself on its warm welcome and its beautifully maintained greens. For those who enjoy the rugged, hilly nature of Borders golf, Langholm is a hidden gem that offers an authentic taste of local history.

Langlands
Langlands Golf Course is a polished 18-hole parkland facility situated on the southern edge of East Kilbride. Operated by South Lanarkshire Leisure, it serves as a premier municipal test that feels significantly more "private" than its status suggests. The course is characterized by its wide, lush fairways and large, receptive greens, making it a favorite for those who enjoy opening their shoulders with the driver. However, the layout is cleverly punctuated by mature woodland and subtle dog-legs that require thoughtful positioning.

The club has a vibrant community spirit and is known for its "Noonies Sweep" a legendary local gathering that fosters a competitive but friendly atmosphere. While it may not host the international professional tours, it is a stalwart of the Lanarkshire county circuit and is frequently praised for its year-round conditioning. The signature par-three 16th, "The Quarry" requires a precise shot over a natural depression and provides a dramatic challenge late in the round. It is a welcoming, high-value course that offers a professional-standard parkland experience for golfers of all levels.

Largs
Largs Golf Club is one of the most picturesque parkland courses in the West of Scotland, offering breathtaking views across the Firth of Clyde to the mountains of Cumbrae and Arran. Founded in 1891, the club moved to its present site within the Kelburn Estate shortly after, playing alongside the 13th-century Kelburn Castle. The course was originally a 9-hole layout designed by Willie Campbell, but it was expanded to a full 18 holes in 1953 with input from local figures, including the legendary coach Bob Torrance.

The club has a prestigious tournament history, recently serving as the host for the 2026 Scottish Men's Area Team Championship. The layout is notoriously tight in places, with mature trees framing the fairways and the Kelburn Burn providing a natural hazard on several holes. Notable holes include the par-four 6th, which dog-legs toward the castle, and the daunting par-four 15th, "Cockleshells." Largs is a refined, aristocratic club that manages to feel both historic and modern, providing an impeccable playing surface and some of the finest scenery in Ayrshire.

Larkhall
Larkhall Golf Club, located adjacent to the M74 motorway, is a resilient 9-hole parkland course that is widely regarded as having some of the best greens in Lanarkshire. Established in 1909, the course is a municipal facility that has long been the sporting heart of the town. While it is a shorter par-35 layout, it offers a varied mix of holes that test both length and accuracy. The course is known for its excellent drainage, often remaining open and playable in the depths of winter when other local tracks are closed.

The club is famous for its inclusive and friendly atmosphere, where "Arlene and the staff" are local legends for their hospitality in the clubhouse. It serves as a vital nursery for the game, with its ACE children's activities introducing generations of local kids to the sport. While it doesn't boast a celebrity architect, the layout is a masterclass in community-led golf, featuring narrow fairways and well-protected greens that demand respect. For those looking for an honest, unpretentious, and highly playable round of golf, Larkhall provides a high-quality experience that punches well above its weight.

Lauder
Lauder Golf Club is a historic 9-hole gem nestled in the heart of the Scottish Borders. Established in 1896, the initial layout was overseen by the two-time Open Champion Willie Park Jr., whose strategic fingerprints are still visible on the course today. Park's design philosophy of "using the land" is evident in the clever routing over the undulating parkland, where natural slopes and hollows dictate the play. The course features 17 separate tees to provide a distinct and varied challenge for those playing a full 18-hole loop.

A remarkable piece of history at Lauder is the course record of 70, set by Willie Park Jr. himself in 1905, which remarkably remained unbeaten for over a century. The course features iconic holes like "The Wood" and "The Quarry" which offer a stern test for the low handicapper while remaining enjoyable for the casual player. It is a "walking-friendly" club with a reputation for being one of the most equitable challenges in the region. For the golfing historian, a trip to Lauder is a chance to play on a canvas designed by one of the game's first true masters in a tranquil Borders setting.

Leadhills
Leadhills Golf Club is a unique and rugged 9-hole course that holds the distinction of being the highest golf course in Scotland, sitting at an elevation of over 1,500 feet. Founded in 1889, the course is situated in the heart of the Lowther Hills, where the thin mountain air makes your ball travel noticeably further. Originally established by the local "natives" of Leadhills and Wanlockhead, the club has a history rooted in the local lead-mining industry and the resilient spirit of the high-country communities.

The course is a raw, moorland experience where the fairways are shared with local sheep and the weather can change in an instant. It is a "community asset" in the truest sense, with free memberships offered to local students and a dedicated band of volunteers maintaining the greens. Notable historical figures like the Rev. Mr. Symington were among the early medal winners. Playing Leadhills is not just about the golf; it's about the sheer exhilaration of the landscape. For any golfer looking to tick off a "bucket list" experience, playing at the roof of Scotland is an absolute must.

Leitfie
Leitfie Links is the charming 9-hole sister course at the Strathmore Golf Centre near Alyth. While its larger sibling, Rannaleroch, draws the championship crowds, Leitfie is a high-quality "short course" designed to hone the iron play and short game of all golfers. Measuring just over 1,100 yards with a par of 27, it consists of nine par-threes that range from 92 to 157 yards. Far from being a "pitch and putt" Leitfie is built to the same exacting standards as a championship course, with manicured greens and strategic bunkering.

The course is the perfect "warm-up" venue or a place for beginners to learn the etiquette and basics of the game without the pressure of a long course. It is particularly famous for its 9th green, which features two distinct levels that will test even the most confident putter. The facility is a hub of modern golf, featuring a professional academy and excellent catering. For families or those with limited time, Leitfie Links offers a high-speed, high-quality golfing experience that proves you don't need par-fives to have a memorable day on the links.

Lenzie
Lenzie Golf Club is a classic parkland course situated in East Dunbartonshire, just north of Glasgow. Founded in 1889 as a 9-hole layout, it was extended to its current 18-hole form in 1907. The course is characterized by its lush fairways and an impressive collection of mature trees that provide both shelter and a significant tactical challenge. From the highest point of the course at the 10th tee, players are rewarded with panoramic views of the Campsie Hills and, on clear days, the distant peak of Ben Lomond.

A unique feature of Lenzie is its four par-three holes, which are cleverly routed to face different points of the compass, ensuring that the prevailing wind affects each one differently. The club has a long history of competitive success and is a regular host of regional Glasgow and Dunbartonshire tournaments. Under the guidance of PGA professionals like Alan Waugh, the club has maintained a high standard of tuition and playability. It is a traditional, welcoming club where the quality of the greens and the friendliness of the clubhouse have made it a favorite for over 130 years.

Leslie
Leslie Golf Club in Fife is a historic 9-hole parkland course that acts as a living link to the "Golden Age" of golf architecture. Founded in 1898, the course was designed by the legendary Old Tom Morris in collaboration with Charles Anderson. Despite its modest length, Leslie is notoriously difficult, featuring small, plateau greens that require surgical precision with the short irons. It has remained largely unchanged for over 125 years, offering a pure and nostalgic look at how the game was played at the turn of the century.

The course's signature 2nd hole was once described by legendary broadcaster Peter Alliss as "probably the most difficult hole in world golf"—a narrow, 423-yard par-four that requires a nerve-wracking drive to a "bottle" green. The club is a pioneer in modern recreational golf, being one of the first in the region to incorporate a 9-hole FootGolf course alongside the traditional layout. It is a community-focused club with a popular café, Susan's Kitchen, providing a warm welcome. For those who want to experience the strategic genius of Old Tom Morris on a compact and affordable canvas, Leslie is a true hidden gem.

Leven
Leven Links is a giant of the Scottish game and one of the oldest and purest links courses in the world. Originally founded in 1846, the course took its modern 18-hole form in 1868 following input from Old Tom Morris. To celebrate its opening, an exhibition match was held and won by the 17-year-old Young Tom Morris, the greatest player of his era. It is a classic links characterized by ancient sand dunes, deep pot bunkers, and the famous "Scoonie Burn" that guards the final green.

The course has an immense tournament pedigree, having served as a Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship on six occasions between 1978 and 2005. It remains a regular host for the Standard Life Leven Gold Medal, the world's oldest amateur stroke-play tournament. Leven is a "thinking man's links" where the bump-and-run shot is an essential tool, especially during the dry summer months. Shared between the Leven Thistle Golf Club and the Leven Golfing Society, the course represents the absolute pinnacle of traditional Fife links golf, offering a historic and uncompromising test of skill.

Liberton
Liberton Golf Club, located just four miles from Edinburgh city center, is a charming parkland course that made history upon its founding in 1920 as one of the first clubs in Britain to put male and female members on an equal footing. The course is situated on 89 acres of undulating land and, despite its relatively short length of 5,344 yards, it is widely respected as a "card-wrecker" due to its tight, tree-lined fairways and exceptionally small greens guarded by deep bunkers.

The club has a proud competitive history, frequently hosting the Lothians Seniors and Junior Championships. A notable figure in the club's history is W. Dakers, a former club champion who served as the Secretary of the Scottish Golf Union for 25 years. Under the guidance of PGA professional Iain Seath, the club has developed excellent practice facilities, including a dedicated pitching and chipping area. Liberton is a friendly, progressive club that offers a tactical and refined round of golf, where accuracy is rewarded far more than brute force, making it a favorite for the capital's golfers.

Lilliardsedge
Lilliardsedge Golf Club is a hidden gem tucked away within the rolling hills of the Scottish Borders. Originally a 9-hole layout, the course now utilizes 18 separate tees to create a varied and challenging 5,299-yard experience. Set within a beautifully landscaped holiday park, the course feels significantly more established than its modern origins suggest. The layout is characterized by its natural water features, including a central lake and several winding burns that come into play on the most critical approach shots.


While the course does not have a "celebrity" architect attached to its name, it is a masterclass in modern, community-focused design. The greens are notoriously tricky, featuring subtle slopes that require a sharp eye. The club is a popular venue for the Borders Golfers' Association and maintains a high standard of conditioning year-round. It is a friendly, relaxed environment where the tavern at the turn offers some of the best golfing hospitality in the region. For those touring the south of Scotland, Lilliardsedge provides a peaceful and scenic round of golf that rewards accuracy and strategic placement over raw distance.

Lilliesleaf
Lilliesleaf Golf Course, also known as Dimpleknowe, is a quirky and charismatic 11-hole course that offers a refreshing alternative to the traditional 18-hole format. Situated on a private country estate in the heart of the Borders, the course measures a testing 2,563 yards for its primary loop. It is a "fun" course in the truest sense—the dress code is informal, children and dogs are welcomed, and the focus is firmly on the enjoyment of the game. Despite its relaxed atmosphere, the layout features genuine challenges, including a testing par-five and several tight par-threes.


The course is famous for its natural, unpretentious beauty, with fairways that wind through mature woodland and over natural slopes. There is no "famous" architect here; instead, the course has been lovingly shaped by the estate owners to reflect the rugged charm of the surrounding landscape. The club recently added a covered BBQ area near the starter's hut, making it a favorite for family outings and social groups. It is an honest, traditional Scottish "village" course where the spirit of the game is kept alive through community passion and a total lack of pretense.

Linlithgow
Linlithgow Golf Club is a magnificent parkland course that celebrated its centenary in 2013. The original layout was designed in 1913 by Robert Simpson, the legendary professional at Carnoustie, and four of his original holes still survive today. The course was eventually expanded to 18 holes in the 1950s thanks to the patronage of the MacKinnon family (of Drambuie fame), who purchased the surrounding farmland to protect the club's future. Mrs. Gena MacKinnon was a beloved figure at the club, famously performing the opening ceremony every year for decades.

The course is characterized by its dramatic elevation changes and panoramic views over the Forth Valley and the Ochil Hills. It is a tactical test where the "braehead" winds often dictate play. Notable tournaments include the West Lothian County Championships, and the club has a reputation for producing high-caliber amateur talent. The signature holes along the ridge require nerve and a steady hand. With its deep connection to Scottish industry and its classic Simpson-influenced design, Linlithgow offers a polished and historic golfing experience in the shadow of the royal burgh's famous palace.

Littlehill
Littlehill Golf Course is a historic 18-hole parkland gem located just north of Glasgow city center. Opened in 1923, the course was designed by the most prolific architect in history, James Braid. Littlehill carries a significant championship pedigree, having served as an Open Championship Qualifying venue in the mid-20th century. Braid utilized the relatively flat terrain to create a layout that emphasizes strategic bunkering and large, subtly sloping greens, making it a fair but formidable test for golfers of all abilities.


As a municipal course, Littlehill has been a vital training ground for generations of Glasgow golfers. Its lush fairways are lined with a diverse range of mature trees, providing a sense of seclusion despite its proximity to the city. The course is famously well-maintained, with its greens often cited as some of the fastest in the municipal circuit. Notable for its friendly, unpretentious atmosphere, Littlehill represents the "Braid" philosophy of accessible, high-quality golf for the people. It remains a popular choice for those seeking a historic and well-conditioned round of golf without the formality of a private estate.

Lochcarron
Lochcarron Golf Club offers one of the most cinematic 9-hole experiences in the Scottish Highlands. Founded in 1908 on the shores of Wester Ross, the course is surrounded by the dramatic peaks of the Strathcarron mountains. Little has changed in its layout over the last century, with the shores of the loch serving as a natural (and terminal) boundary for many holes. The names of the holes, such as "Churchyard" and "Burnside" reflect the course's deep roots in the local community.


While it lacks a professional "name" architect, the layout is a masterpiece of natural design, with the routing dictated by the rugged shoreline and the surrounding hills. The club recently secured its future by purchasing the land and building a modern clubhouse that offers some of the finest views in the Highlands. It is an "honesty box" course that rewards the adventurous golfer with a pure, raw connection to the origins of the game. For those traveling the North Coast 500, a stop at Lochcarron is a mandatory pilgrimage to play a course that feels as though it was carved out of the landscape by the elements themselves.

Lochgelly
Lochgelly Golf Club is a rugged and rewarding parkland course in the heart of Fife. Established in 1895, the course was originally a 9-hole layout before being extended to 18 holes in the 1920s with input from the legendary James Braid. The course is built on a series of natural ridges, providing a physical test and offering expansive views across the Forth toward Edinburgh. It is a course with an "industrial heart" founded by local miners who shaped the land with their own hands.

The layout is known for its firm, well-draining turf and its challenging par-fives that require both power and precision. Notable tournaments held here include the Fife County Championships, and the club has a reputation for its fiercely competitive but welcoming membership. The signature "ridge holes" demand a creative approach, as the wind often sweeps across the exposed fairways. It is a traditional, no-nonsense Scottish club where the quality of the golf and the warmth of the hospitality are the primary attractions. For those seeking an authentic and challenging Fife parkland experience, Lochgelly is a hidden gem that punches well above its weight.

Lochgilphead
Lochgilphead Golf Club is a delightful 9-hole course situated at the head of Loch Gilp in Argyll. Established in 1891, the course was refined in the early 20th century by James Braid, who was a frequent visitor to the region. Braid's influence is seen in the clever use of the undulating terrain and the placement of several strategic hazards that protect the small, fast greens. The course offers spectacular views over the loch and the surrounding mountains of Knapdale.

The club is a focal point of the local community and is known for its "open" and inclusive atmosphere. While it may be short in yardage, the course is notoriously difficult to play to your handicap, as the natural slopes often funnel well-struck shots toward the rough. It has produced many fine local champions and is a regular venue for Argyll and Bute regional events. The signature par-three holes require surgical precision with the short irons. It is a quintessential West Highland course—rugged, scenic, and deeply traditional—offering a tranquil and engaging round of golf in one of the most beautiful corners of the country.

Lochgoilhead
Lochgoilhead Golf Club is a stunningly located 9-hole course set within the Drimsynie Estate in the Cowal Peninsula. Originally laid out in the 1970s to complement the local holiday resort, the course has matured into a scenic parkland layout that provides a stern test of accuracy. It is surrounded by the dramatic peaks of the "Arrochar Alps" and the views from the tees are among the most photographed in the region. The course is characterized by its steep elevation changes and narrow, tree-lined fairways.

While it lacks a historic architect, the layout is a clever use of a challenging site, requiring golfers to navigate natural gullies and rocky outcrops. The club has a relaxed, resort-style atmosphere, making it a favorite for families and visitors. However, the greens are remarkably well-maintained and can be quite slick in the summer months. Notable for its peace and seclusion, Lochgoilhead offers a "mountain golf" experience where the scenery is as much a part of the challenge as the hazards themselves. It is a perfect example of how modern recreational design can harmonize with a wild and ancient landscape.

Loch Lomond
Loch Lomond Golf Club is a world-renowned masterpiece of parkland design. Opened in 1993, the course was designed by the legendary partnership of Tom Weiskopf and Jay Morrish. It is consistently ranked as one of the top 100 courses in the world and served as the long-time home of the Scottish Open on the European Tour. The course is set within the ancestral lands of the Colquhoun Clan, with the 18th-century Rossdhu House serving as the most magnificent clubhouse in the sport.


The club is notoriously exclusive, with a membership that includes global titans of industry and major-winning professionals. Each hole is entirely secluded from the next, winding through ancient woodland and along the "bonnie banks" of the loch. Notable members have included Sir Jackie Stewart and Sean Connery. The ruins of Rossdhu Castle overlook the 18th green, providing a hauntingly beautiful backdrop to the finishing hole. To play Loch Lomond is to experience the absolute pinnacle of luxury golf—an impeccably manicured sanctuary where history, nature, and world-class architecture meet.

Lochmaben
Lochmaben Golf Club is a historic parkland course in Dumfriesshire with a layout of immense archaeological significance. Founded in 1926, the original 9-hole course was designed by the five-time Open Champion James Braid. The course is built on the site of one of King Robert the Bruce's first castles, and the mottes of the ancient fortress are clearly visible on the 2nd and 4th holes. The layout was eventually expanded to 18 holes, creating a scenic and testing circuit that encircles the Kirk Loch.


The course is famous for its "Swan's Nest" hole, a dramatic dog-leg where the water of the loch sits just yards behind the green. Notable tournaments include the South of Scotland Championships, and the club is highly regarded for its fast-running turf and immaculate conditioning. The connection to the Bruce family is central to the club's identity, with the town's motto, "From us is born the liberator king" proudly displayed in the clubhouse. It is a friendly, scenic, and intellectually stimulating course that offers a unique blend of championship-standard golf and ancient Scottish history.

Loch Ness - Fairways Golf Centre
Loch Ness Golf Club, specifically the Fairways Golf Centre, provides a modern and inclusive approach to the game in the capital of the Highlands. Opened in the late 1990s, the facility features a championship-length 18-hole course alongside a shorter, family-friendly 9-hole layout. The main course is a lush parkland design that offers expansive views of the surrounding hills. It was designed to be a "fair" test—wide enough to accommodate the casual golfer but long enough to challenge the local low-handicappers.

The centre is a hub for golf development in the North, boasting a state-of-the-art driving range and a popular golf academy. While it may not have the ancient pedigree of its coastal neighbors, it has become a staple for regional PGA Scotland events. Notable for its relaxed atmosphere and lack of stuffy traditions, Fairways is where many of Inverness's rising stars first gripped a club. The par-four finishing hole, requiring a carry over water to a green overlooked by the clubhouse balcony, provides a theatrical end to a round that highlights the growth of modern Scottish golf.

Lochore Meadows
Lochore Meadows Golf Course is a scenic 9-hole parkland layout situated within a massive country park in Fife. Developed on the site of a former colliery, the course is a triumph of land reclamation, transforming an industrial landscape into a tranquil sporting haven. Opened in the 1980s, the course wraps around the shores of Loch Ore, with several holes offering stunning water views. The layout is relatively flat, making it an accessible walk, but mature tree-planting and strategic bunkers ensure it remains a respectable test.


The club is a vital community asset, serving as a primary venue for the Fife Municipal Golf Association. It is particularly famous for its abundant wildlife; it is not uncommon to see deer or ospreys while lining up a putt. While it does not boast a celebrity architect, the routing is praised for its logical flow and its ability to drain well during the wet Scottish winters. It is a "people's course" in the best sense, offering high-quality golf at a very affordable price, proving that the heart of the Scottish game often beats strongest in these reclaimed community spaces.

Lochranza
Located at the northern tip of the Isle of Arran, Lochranza Golf Course is a 9-hole moorland and parkland hybrid that offers a truly unique Highland experience. Surrounded by dramatic mountains and the ruins of a 13th-century castle, the course is as much a nature reserve as it is a sporting venue. It is famously the home of a massive herd of red deer, who are so accustomed to golfers that they often remain lounging on the fairways while players hit their shots—a local rule dictates that a ball coming to rest near a deer may be dropped without penalty!

The course is natural and unpretentious, reflecting the wild beauty of its surroundings. There is no "famous" architect here; instead, the terrain dictates the play, with natural burns and steep banks serving as the primary hazards. The club is a favorite for visitors staying at the nearby youth hostel or distillery, offering a relaxed and incredibly atmospheric round. Playing at Lochranza is less about the technicalities of the swing and more about the spiritual connection to the Scottish landscape, making it a "must-play" for any golfing romantic.

Lochview
Lochview Family Golf Centre, located in Coatbridge, is a premier municipal facility that has become a cornerstone of golf in North Lanarkshire. The 18-hole parkland course is designed with playability in mind, featuring wide fairways and large, consistent greens. As the name suggests, the course offers pleasant views over the local loch and parkland. It was developed to provide a high-quality, professional environment for the general public, and it succeeds through meticulous maintenance and excellent practice facilities.

The centre is a pioneer in the "Golf for All" movement, featuring a floodlit driving range and a comprehensive junior program that has introduced thousands of local children to the sport. While it doesn't host the professional majors, it is a frequent venue for Lanarkshire County junior events. The layout is strategic without being punishing, rewarding those who can navigate the gentle undulations of the Lanarkshire countryside. For many in the central belt, Lochview represents the ideal balance of affordability, quality, and a welcoming, modern club atmosphere.

Lochwinnoch
Lochwinnoch Golf Club is a spectacular 18-hole parkland-moorland hybrid perched on the hills overlooking the Renfrewshire countryside. Founded in 1923, the course was significantly redesigned by the esteemed James Braid in the late 1920s. Braid's genius is evident in the way the course utilizes the steep terrain to create a variety of challenging shots, including several dramatic elevated tees. On a clear day, the views from the higher holes stretch across the Clyde to the mountains of the southern Highlands.

The club has a reputation for being one of the friendliest in the region, with a lively social scene. Notable for its impeccably fast greens, the course is a regular host for the Renfrewshire County Championships. The signature 12th hole, a par-three played from a high tee to a green guarded by a burn, is one of the most memorable in the county. While the climb can be physically demanding, the reward is a high-quality, historic Braid layout that offers a tranquil escape from the urban hustle of nearby Glasgow.

Lockerbie
Lockerbie Golf Club is a beautiful parkland course in Dumfriesshire with a layout that dates back to 1889. The course was significantly refined by the legendary James Braid in the 1920s, who turned it into a challenging 18-hole test that winds through mature woodland and over natural slopes. Braid's influence is seen in the well-placed bunkering and the trueness of the greens. The course offers a peaceful, sheltered environment with views stretching toward the Lake District on a clear day.

The club has a proud history and was famously a favorite of the local community who rebuilt the clubhouse following the tragedy of 1988, turning it into a symbol of resilience and hospitality. Notable tournaments held here include the South of Scotland Amateur Championship. The course is known for its "hidden" challenges—small streams and subtle dog-legs that punish the overly aggressive player. It is a traditional, unpretentious club where the quality of the playing surface is matched by the warmth of the welcome, representing the very best of Borders parkland golf.

Longniddry
Longniddry Golf Club is a unique and prestigious course on the East Lothian "Golf Coast." Founded in 1921, it was designed by the esteemed Harry Colt, one of the most famous architects in the history of the game. Longniddry is a rare hybrid, blending the characteristics of a traditional links with the shelter and trees of a woodland course. It famously has no par-fives, meaning its par of 68 is one of the toughest to beat in the country. It has frequently served as an Open Championship Qualifying venue.


The course has a high-society pedigree and has counted many prominent figures from Edinburgh's legal and medical circles among its members. Notable visitors have included Sir Henry Cotton, who praised the strategic demands of Colt's design. The layout is notorious for its sea breezes and its fast, undulating greens. Because there are no par-fives, accuracy and a sharp short game are absolutely paramount. Longniddry offers a refined, aristocratic experience that bridges the gap between the wild links of Gullane and the manicured parklands of Edinburgh, making it an essential stop for the serious golfer.

Longside
Longside Golf Club, located in the heart of the Buchan countryside in Aberdeenshire, is a relatively modern 18-hole parkland course that has quickly built a reputation for its conditioning. Opened in 1979 as a 9-hole course and extended to 18 in 1996, the layout was designed by the local committee to provide a testing alternative to the nearby links. The course is characterized by its wide-open spaces and the South Ugie Water, which meanders through the property and provides a natural hazard on no fewer than seven holes.

The club is a focal point for the local agricultural community and is known for its incredibly relaxed and welcoming atmosphere. While it lacks a "celebrity" architect, the layout is highly regarded for its clever use of the water and the prevailing North Sea winds. The greens are often cited as some of the most consistent in the North East. Longside is a frequent host of Aberdeenshire County junior events, reflecting its commitment to growing the game. For those looking for an honest, wind-swept parkland challenge in a peaceful rural setting, Longside offers a high-value and enjoyable round of golf.

Loudoun Gowf
Loudoun Gowf Club is a historic and unique parkland gem in Ayrshire. The club uses the archaic spelling "Gowf" reflecting its deep roots in Scottish tradition; while founded in 1908, golf was played in the area for centuries before. The course was designed by the 1883 Open Champion Willie Fernie and refined by the legendary James Braid. It is set within the magnificent grounds of Loudoun Castle, and the ancient trees and stone walls of the estate provide a stately atmosphere throughout the round.
