The course is famously flat, making it an enjoyable walk, but its defense lies in its incredibly narrow fairways and small, well-protected greens. Notable members over the years have included various Ayrshire dignitaries and sporting legends. Loudoun has hosted many prestigious amateur events, including the Ayrshire Strokeplay Championship. The 18th hole, finishing right in front of the traditional clubhouse, is a highlight. It is a refined, "gentleman's" club that prioritizes precision and etiquette, offering a polished parkland experience steeped in the history of the Ayrshire aristocracy.

Luffness
Luffness New Golf Club is a titan of East Lothian golf and one of the most traditional clubs in the world. Founded in 1894, the course was designed by Old Tom Morris, and it remains a pure, unadulterated links experience. Luffness is famous for its lack of modern "clutter"—there are no yardage markers, no flashy signage, and a strict adherence to traditional dress codes and etiquette. It has a formidable tournament pedigree, having served as a Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship for decades.

The course is a sanctuary of fast-running turf, deep revetted bunkers, and thick, punishing rough. Notable members have included legends of the game and members of the British establishment who value its privacy and tranquility. Unlike the hilly Gullane nearby, Luffness is relatively flat, but the wind is a constant and brutal defender of par. The clubhouse is a time capsule of 19th-century golfing elegance, and the lunch is as famous as the greens. To play Luffness is to step back into the "Golden Age" of golf, offering a quiet, serious, and immensely rewarding links challenge.

Lumphanan
Lumphanan Golf Club is a captivating 9-hole course in Aberdeenshire that provides a classic "Highland" golfing experience. Founded in 1923, the course is perched on the slopes of the Marr area, offering panoramic views of the surrounding mountains and valleys. It is a physically engaging course, characterized by its significant elevation changes and the lush, springy turf typical of the region. The layout was designed to work with the natural contours of the hill, meaning level lies are a luxury rather than a guarantee.

The club is a vibrant hub for the local community and is known for its incredibly warm and unpretentious welcome. While it may not have a "celebrity" architect, the clever routing makes excellent use of natural hazards like the winding burns and granite outcrops. The signature hole, "The Coffin" requires a precise shot over a deep hollow to a green perched on a plateau. Lumphanan is the quintessential village club—peaceful, scenic, and deeply traditional. It offers a high-value round of golf for those who appreciate the raw beauty of the North East countryside and the challenge of mountain-style play.

Lundin Links
Lundin Links is a powerhouse of Scottish golf and a perennial Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship. Originally founded in 1868, the course was redesigned in 1909 by the legendary James Braid after the club split with its neighbor, Leven. Braid utilized the dramatic, undulating links land and the adjacent higher ground to create a hybrid masterpiece. The first five holes are pure links, the middle section offers a more elevated "parkland" feel with spectacular views over the Firth of Forth, and the finishing stretch returns to the classic dunes.

The club has hosted almost every major amateur championship in Scotland and has seen legends like Gene Sarazen and Gary Player grace its fairways. Notable for its narrow fairways, deep bunkers, and the "Miles Burn" that crosses the course twice, it is a formidable test of accuracy and nerve. The clubhouse is an architectural gem, and the sense of history is palpable. Lundin Links is a "pure" golfer's course—historic, challenging, and impeccably maintained—offering one of the most varied and rewarding links experiences in Fife.

Lundin Ladies
The Lundin Ladies' Golf Club holds a unique and prestigious place in the history of the game as the oldest women's golf club in the world, founded in 1891. Situated just inland from the famous championship links, this 9-hole course was designed by James Braid in 1910. While the club was founded for women, it is now a welcoming destination for all, but it retains its traditional, intimate charm. The course is a beautiful parkland-links hybrid, featuring small, tricky greens and several significant natural hazards, including the ancient Standing Stones of Lundin.

The course is famously well-manicured and provides a shorter, more tactical alternative to the punishing championship tracks nearby. Notable members have included pioneers of women's golf who fought for the right to play on equal terms. Despite its modest yardage, the course is notoriously difficult to "master" due to the precision required on the approach shots. It is a peaceful, historic sanctuary that offers a relaxed and incredibly atmospheric round. For the golfing historian, a visit to Lundin Ladies is a mandatory pilgrimage to experience the roots of the inclusive modern game in a setting of timeless beauty.

Lybster
Lybster Golf Club is a hidden gem of the far North, located on the rugged coast of Caithness. This 9-hole course is one of the shortest in Scotland, but what it lacks in distance, it more than makes up for in character and challenge. Founded in 1905, the course is situated on a cliff-top site overlooking the North Sea. The layout is a raw, natural links experience where the wind is the primary architect and defender. On a clear day, the views stretch across the Moray Firth to the hills of Aberdeenshire.

The club is an "honesty box" facility, maintained by a dedicated group of local volunteers who take immense pride in their small piece of golfing heaven. The greens are remarkably true, and the fairways are lined with gorse and wildflowers. One of the most famous features is the proximity to the historic Grey Cairns of Camster and the local "Whaligoe Steps." It is a place of total solitude, where the only sound is the crashing of the waves against the cliffs. For those traveling the North Coast 500, Lybster offers a pure, unadulterated connection to the origins of Scottish golf in a wild and stunning landscape.

Murrayshall - Lynedoch
The Lynedoch Course at Murrayshall Country Estate near Perth is a high-quality parkland test that complements the main championship course. Opened in 2000 and designed by Graeme Webster, the Lynedoch is a shorter, tighter, and more technical layout that winds through towering Scots pine and mature woodland. Webster utilized the natural undulations of the estate to create a "target golf" experience where accuracy from the tee is far more important than raw power.

The course has a modern, professional feel and is maintained to an exceptionally high standard. It is a frequent venue for regional PGA Scotland events and serves as an excellent challenge for those looking to hone their iron play. The greens are notoriously fast and feature subtle breaks that demand a sharp eye. With the backdrop of the Perthshire hills and the luxury of the estate hotel, the Lynedoch offers a polished and secluded golfing experience. It is the perfect choice for a second round of the day, providing a varied and engaging contrast to the more expansive championship layout at the resort.

The Machrie
The Machrie, located on the Isle of Islay, is a legendary links that sits among the upper echelons of world golf. Originally designed in 1891 by Willie Campbell, the course underwent a total transformation in 2018 by architect David J. Russell. The redesign modernized the layout while preserving the soul of its ancient, towering dunes. It is a pure links experience, characterized by massive elevation changes, hidden hollows, and panoramic views of the Atlantic Ocean and the Big Strand beach.

The course has a storied history, once hosting an exhibition match in 1901 with a prize fund that rivaled the Open Championship. Notable visitors include every serious student of links golf, drawn by its reputation for raw beauty and tactical complexity. The turf is firm and fast-running, and the wind is a constant, shifting factor. The modern hotel and clubhouse have turned The Machrie into a world-class destination. To play here is to experience golf in its most elemental form—a wild, spectacular, and deeply moving journey across one of the most historic pieces of links land in existence.

Machrie Bay
On the opposite side of the island from its world-famous namesake, Machrie Bay on the Isle of Arran is a delightful 9-hole course that offers a completely different experience. Established in 1900, the course is a traditional, family-run links that sits right on the shoreline. It is a flat, easy-to-walk course that emphasizes fun and accessibility. The layout is natural and unpretentious, with the sea coming into play on several holes and the dramatic peaks of Arran providing a stunning backdrop.

The club is famous for its relaxed atmosphere and its excellent tearoom, which is a destination in its own right for locals and visitors alike. While it lacks a "celebrity" architect, the course has a unique charm, featuring small greens and narrow fairways that require a steady hand. It is a favorite for families and for those looking for a quick, scenic round before heading to the nearby distillery. Machrie Bay represents the "joy of the game"—a place where the focus is on the beauty of the surroundings and the companionship of the round. It is a quintessential Scottish island course that leaves every visitor with a smile.

Machrihanish
Machrihanish Golf Club is a titan of the game, home to what is widely considered the greatest opening hole in world golf. Designed by the "Grand Old Man of Golf" Old Tom Morris, in 1876, the course is a pure links situated on the remote Kintyre Peninsula. The 1st hole, "Battery" requires a heroic drive over the Atlantic beach to find the fairway—a shot that has challenged every great player from Babe Zaharias to Jack Nicklaus. It is an uncompromising and atmospheric test of links golf.

The course is a sanctuary of natural dunes, hidden hollows, and lightning-fast greens. It has a prestigious tournament history, frequently hosting the Scottish Ladies' Championship and regional qualifying for major events. Notable for its "out and back" routing, the course takes players deep into the wild dunes of Machrihanish Bay. The sense of isolation and the raw power of the sea make playing here a spiritual experience for many. It is a course that rewards creativity and a low ball flight. For any serious golfer, a trip to Machrihanish is a pilgrimage to one of the most hallowed and beautiful sites in the history of the sport.

Machrihanish Dunes
Machrihanish Dunes is a revolutionary modern links that sits adjacent to the historic Old Tom Morris course. Opened in 2009 and designed by David McLay Kidd (the architect behind Bandon Dunes), it is the first course ever built on a site of Special Scientific Interest (SSSI). As such, the design was "found" rather than built; no heavy machinery was used to shape the fairways, and the layout follows the natural, rugged contours of the dunes exactly as they have sat for centuries.

The course is a wild, adventurous experience, featuring massive, undulating greens and deep natural blow-outs. It is notoriously difficult, especially when the wind whips off the Atlantic, requiring a huge variety of shots and a high degree of patience. Notable visitors include modern stars of the game who appreciate the "raw" and ecological approach to design. The Dunes offers a fascinating contrast to its neighbor, providing a much more rugged and unpredictable round. It is a testament to sustainable design and a "must-play" for those who want to see how the origins of links golf are being reimagined for the 21st century.

Mains of Taymouth
Mains of Taymouth, located in the heart of Kenmore at the tip of Loch Tay, is often described as the "finest 9-hole course in Perthshire." Opened in 1992, the course was designed to complement the luxury holiday estate and offers a high-quality parkland experience. The layout is set on a hillside, providing spectacular views over the loch and the surrounding mountains of the Tay Valley. It is characterized by its large, undulating greens and several significant water hazards that require strategic navigation.

The course has a modern, resort-style feel and is maintained to an impeccable standard. While it is a shorter course, the elevation changes and the narrow, tree-lined fairways provide a stern test of accuracy. It is a popular venue for visitors staying in the estate's luxury cottages and has built a strong reputation for its friendly, professional atmosphere. The signature 9th hole requires an approach over a pond to a green overlooked by the clubhouse balcony. Mains of Taymouth is a perfect example of a high-end "boutique" golf course, offering a peaceful and scenic round in one of Scotland's most beautiful lochside settings.

Mar Hall
Mar Hall Golf & Spa Resort is home to the Earl of Mar course, a five-star championship parkland layout that graces the banks of the River Clyde. Designed by the renowned architect Dave Thomas—the man behind the Brabazon at The Belfry—the course was carved out of 240 acres of ancient wooded estate in 2010. Thomas skillfully incorporated the estate's 250-year-old chestnut and beech trees into the routing, creating a course that feels far older than its years.


The resort has a high-society pedigree, with the 19th-century gothic mansion (designed by Sir Robert Smirke) serving as one of Scotland's most luxurious clubhouses. Famous guests have included A-list celebrities and world-class athletes, from Lionel Messi to Kylie Minogue, who have sought the privacy and elegance of the estate. While the course provides a stern test for the low-handicapper with its strategic bunkering and water hazards, it remains accessible for guests looking for a relaxed round. Notable tournaments often include high-end corporate events and regional PGA outings, all held against the dramatic backdrop of the Kilpatrick Hills and the Erskine Bridge.


Maverston
Maverston Golf Club, located near Urquhart in Moray, is a tranquil Highland retreat that offers a "hidden gem" experience. The 18-hole Maverston Course was designed by Euan McIntosh, a former Scottish Internationalist and a formidable name in the amateur game. Opened in 2014, the course takes advantage of the gently undulating landscape of the Maverston Highland Estate. It is a modern parkland layout characterized by its wide-open vistas, offering views of the Garmouth hills to the south and the Moray Firth to the north.


The club is known for its inclusive and welcoming atmosphere, breaking away from some of the more rigid traditions of older Scottish institutions. While it is a young course, it has quickly gained a reputation for its conditioning and the trueness of its greens. It frequently hosts regional Texas Scramble Opens and Seniors events, drawing players from across the North East. For those seeking a peaceful, scenic round where the air is fresh and the pace of play is unhurried, Maverston provides a high-quality alternative to the crowded coastal links of the region.

Maverston - Kempston
The Kempston Course at Maverston is the club's delightful 9-hole sibling, providing a shorter but no less engaging challenge. Also designed by Euan McIntosh, the Kempston is a par-three course that serves as the perfect training ground for juniors and beginners, or a high-speed "short game" sharpen-up for experienced players. Despite its shorter yardage, the course is maintained to the same exacting standards as the main championship track, featuring manicured greens and clever bunkering.


The Kempston is a vital part of the club's community-first philosophy, often serving as the gateway for local families and new golfers to enter the sport. It offers a relaxed environment where the technicalities of iron play are the primary focus. Many members use the Kempston for a quick evening round when time is short, enjoying the same spectacular Highland scenery that defines the estate. It is a masterclass in how a modern facility can cater to all levels of the game, ensuring that the "home of golf" remains accessible to the next generation.

Maybole
Maybole Golf Course is a charming 9-hole parkland course situated in the "Kingdom of Carrick" in South Ayrshire. Set within the town's Memorial Park, the course offers uninterrupted views of the southern uplands and the Carrick Hills. While many Ayrshire courses are defined by their brutal links land, Maybole is a more sheltered and welcoming experience. It is part of the Golf South Ayrshire stable, which includes the legendary James Braid-designed courses, and it maintains a high standard of public-sector conditioning.

The layout is notoriously "friendly" with fewer hazards and more generous fairways than its coastal neighbors, making it a favorite for beginners and senior golfers. However, the gentle rolling terrain requires a bit of local knowledge to master the slopes. The club is a pillar of the local community, and its professional staff are often involved in regional development programs. For those following the "Braid Trail" or exploring the historic heart of Ayrshire, Maybole provides a tranquil and affordable "stroll in the park" that captures the unpretentious spirit of Scottish municipal golf.

McDonald
McDonald Golf Club in Ellon is one of Aberdeenshire's most beloved parkland treasures. Established in 1926, the course was gifted to the people of Ellon by the local benefactor Sir James McDonald. The original 9-hole layout was carved out of ancient woodland, and the course was eventually expanded to a full 18 holes that wind through majestic forests of rhododendrons and towering hardwoods. The front nine is famously defined by a meandering burn that twists across the fairways and comes into play on nearly every hole.


The club has a prestigious history in the North East and has produced several notable amateur champions. One of its most famous features is the "red squirrel sanctuary" status of the woods; a quiet golfer is often treated to a sighting of the native wildlife during their round. Notable tournaments held here include the Aberdeenshire County Championships. The back nine, in particular, is often cited by locals as one of the most stunning stretches of parkland golf in the country. It is a social, vibrant club that prides itself on maintaining a "private estate" feel while remaining a warm and inclusive community hub.

Mearns Castle
Mearns Castle Golf Academy, located just south of Glasgow, is a premier 9-hole parkland course and training facility that has redefined the "academy" experience in Scotland. The course is built around the ruins of the 15th-century Mearns Castle, providing a dramatic historical backdrop to the first few holes. In 2015, the routing was modernized to create a more challenging and strategic finish, breaking up the par-threes to ensure a varied test of golf.

The facility is perhaps most famous for its world-class practice amenities, including a state-of-the-art Toptracer driving range and short-game area. It is a frequent haunt for local PGA professionals and has been a nursery for several of the West of Scotland's rising amateur stars. While the course is public, it maintains a highly competitive membership and hosts regular medals. The signature views across the city toward the Campsies make it a scenic escape. Mearns Castle is the ideal destination for the "busy" modern golfer who wants a high-quality, high-tech experience that doesn't sacrifice the beauty and challenge of traditional parkland play.

Meldrum House
Meldrum House Country House & Golf Club is the epitome of Scottish parkland luxury. Designed by the esteemed architect Graeme Webster and opened in 1998, the course sits within a private 240-acre estate in Aberdeenshire. It is one of the few courses in Scotland that can be stretched to over 7,000 yards from the back tees, making it a formidable "championship" test. The layout features USGA-spec greens, allowing for world-class playing surfaces twelve months of the year.

The club is famously exclusive but offers a refined welcome to visitors staying at the 800-year-old manor house hotel. Notable features include the "18 bunkers" that guard the green at the par-five 11th hole—a sight that has intimidated even the best professional players. Famous guests and members have included international businessmen and sports stars who appreciate the club's privacy and high-end service. With its cross-cut fairways, luxury dining domes, and world-class practice facilities, Meldrum House offers a polished, high-gloss golfing experience that rivals the finest resorts in the world.

Melrose
Melrose Golf Club is a rugged and scenic 9-hole course that clings to the base of the iconic Eildon Hills in the Scottish Borders. Founded in 1880, the course is a "mountain goat" experience that requires as much fitness as it does finesse. The original layout was significantly improved in the early 20th century, utilizing the steep natural slopes to create a variety of blind shots and dramatic elevation changes. From the highest point of the course, players are treated to a cinematic view of the historic Melrose Abbey and the Tweed Valley.

The club is a focal point of the local community, especially during the famous Melrose Sevens rugby weekend, when the clubhouse becomes a hub of activity. While it doesn't host professional tours, it is a regular venue for the Borders Golfers' Association championships. The course is known for its firm, moorland turf and its remarkably small, sloping greens. It is a place where local knowledge is king; knowing which side of the hill to land your ball is more important than how far you can hit it. For the adventurous golfer, Melrose offers a traditional, physical, and intensely beautiful round of golf.

Melville
Melville Golf Centre, located on the outskirts of Edinburgh, is a modern and highly accessible 9-hole parkland course that caters to the city's growing golfing population. Opened in the 1990s, the facility was designed by Graeme Webster to provide a high-quality "pay as you play" experience. The course is relatively flat and easy to walk, featuring wide fairways and large greens, making it perfect for beginners or those looking for a stress-free afternoon round.

The centre is particularly renowned for its world-class practice facilities, including a floodlit, heated driving range that is one of the busiest in the Lothians. It has served as a vital entry point for thousands of Edinburgh residents into the sport. While it lacks the ancient pedigree of the city's private clubs, it has become a staple for regional junior events and corporate golf days. The signature hole requires a precise approach over a pond to a well-guarded green. Melville represents the "friendly face" of modern golf—efficient, professional, and entirely focused on making the game as enjoyable as possible for everyone.

Merchants of Edinburgh
The Merchants of Edinburgh Golf Club is a historic and tactical masterpiece situated in the Morningside area of the capital. Originally designed by the famous Ben Sayers in 1907 and later remodelled in 1936 by the legendary James Braid, the course is a model of strategic design on a compact site. Despite its shorter yardage, it is a formidable test of accuracy, featuring steep hills and what is famously one of the few "square" greens in the world.


The club offers unparalleled views across the city, from the Forth Bridges to Edinburgh Castle and the Pentland Hills. Notable members have historically included many of the city's prominent "merchants" and professional elite. Because the course is built on a high ridge, the wind is a constant factor that requires a creative, low ball flight. The clubhouse, rebuilt in 2001, is a modern sanctuary that retains the club's traditional, welcoming character. For those looking for a historic, high-quality, and physically challenging round of golf in the heart of Edinburgh, "The Merchants" is a prestigious and essential stop.

Millport
Millport Golf Club, situated on the charming Isle of Cumbrae, offers a classic moorland experience with some of the most panoramic views in the West of Scotland. Founded in 1888, the course was redesigned in 1913 by the legendary James Braid, who capitalized on the island's natural undulations to create a layout that tests every club in the bag. The course sits high above the town, providing a 360-degree vista that includes the peaks of Arran, the mountains of Argyll, and the Ayrshire coast.


The club has a fiercely loyal membership and a reputation for providing a warm island welcome. While the yardage is modest by modern standards, Braid's strategic bunkering and the ever-present Firth of Clyde breeze ensure that "mastering" Millport is no easy feat. Notable tournaments include the Cumbrae Open, which draws players from the mainland for a day of competitive golf and ferry-hop adventure. The turf is firm and springy, typical of the best moorland tracks. For those seeking a tranquil escape, the short ferry crossing from Largs leads to a course where the scenery is as much a part of the score as the putting.

Milnathort
Milnathort Golf Club is a beautiful 9-hole parkland gem in the heart of Kinross-shire. Established in 1910, the course was originally a modest affair before being significantly refined in the 1920s with input from the esteemed James Braid. The layout is characterized by its lush, mature trees and the North Queich burn, which meanders through the property and provides a natural hazard on several of the most challenging holes. Despite being a 9-hole course, it features 18 distinct tee positions that create a varied and engaging full-round experience.

The club is known for its impeccably fast greens and its family-friendly atmosphere. While it hasn't hosted international professional tours, it is a staple for regional Perth & Kinross county events. The course is relatively flat, making it an enjoyable walk for golfers of all ages, but Braid's clever positioning of hazards ensures that low-handicappers must still show respect. Notable for its "hidden" challenges and excellent conditioning, Milnathort offers a traditional Scottish parkland round in a peaceful, rural setting that feels entirely secluded from the nearby motorway.

Milngavie
Milngavie Golf Club is a premier moorland course located on the edge of the Mugdock Country Park, just north of Glasgow. Founded in 1895, the course was redesigned in 1923 by the great James Braid and later refined by Willie Fernie. The layout is set against the dramatic backdrop of the Campsie Fells and is characterized by its heather-lined fairways and natural rock outcrops. It is a tactical course where accuracy is far more important than length, as Braid utilized the local terrain to punish the overly aggressive player.

The club has a prestigious history and has produced many fine amateur players who have represented Dunbartonshire at the highest levels. Notable for its "hidden" holes and blind shots, Milngavie provides a sense of adventure that is rare in modern parkland golf. The signature 14th hole offers spectacular views across the city toward the distant mountains. The clubhouse is a welcoming sanctuary after a round on the rugged, springy turf. It is a "pure" golf club that has maintained its traditional values while ensuring its course remains a high-quality, championship-standard test.

Minto
Minto Golf Club is a scenic and challenging parkland course situated on the slopes of Minto Hill in the heart of the Scottish Borders. Established in 1928, the course was designed by the local professional Tom Fernie, who created a layout that rewards accuracy and stamina. The course is characterized by its dramatic elevation changes and panoramic views across the Teviot Valley toward the Cheviot Hills. The mature woodland and vibrant gorse provide a colorful and natural environment for a round of golf.

The club is known for its warm Borders hospitality and its remarkably well-conditioned greens. While it lacks the "famous name" architects of the coast, the layout is highly respected for its strategic depth; several holes require shots over natural ravines and through narrow corridors of trees. Notable for its "quiet" beauty, Minto is a regular venue for Borders Golfers' Association tournaments. The 12th hole, "The Pulpit" offers a view from the tee that is worth the green fee alone. It is a traditional, unpretentious club that offers a tranquil and engaging round of golf in one of the most beautiful corners of the south.

Moffat
Moffat Golf Club is a rugged and atmospheric moorland course that offers a truly unique "highland" experience in the Southern Uplands. Founded in 1884, the course was redesigned in 1904 by the legendary Ben Sayers, who utilized the steep hillsides to create a layout that is both physically and mentally demanding. The course sits 800 feet above sea level, providing spectacular views over the Annan Valley and the historic spa town of Moffat.

The club is famous for its "volcanic" greens—small, plateau surfaces that require surgical precision with the short irons. Notable for its lack of artificial bunkers, the course relies on the natural terrain, heather, and wind to defend its par. It is a place where local knowledge is essential, as the slopes can turn a good drive into a difficult lie. The club has a rich social history and was a favorite of the Victorian elite who visited the town for its mineral springs. For those who enjoy the "raw" side of the game, Moffat provides a spirited and historic round of golf in a wild, stunning landscape.

Monifieth - Medal
The Medal Course at Monifieth Links is a titan of Angus golf and a frequent Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship. This traditional links land has a history of golf dating back to at least 1639. The course was originally designed by Old Tom Morris and later refined by James Braid, creating a layout that is both historic and uncompromising. It is famously the place where Tom Watson played his first-ever round of links golf before going on to win the Open at nearby Carnoustie in 1975.

The course is characterized by its deep bunkers, firm fairways, and the railway line that runs parallel to the early holes. Notable for its difficulty when the wind blows off the Tay Estuary, the Medal Course requires a wide variety of shot-making. It has hosted every major amateur championship in Scotland and remains a favorite for low-handicappers seeking a pure links test. The turf is of the highest quality, and the sense of history is palpable from the first tee to the final green. Monifieth Medal is an essential stop for any serious golfer visiting the Dundee area.

Monifieth - Ashludie
The Ashludie Course is the Medal's shorter, quirkier, and arguably more fun younger sibling at Monifieth Links. While it measures just over 5,000 yards, it is no "pushover"; the small, undulating greens and tight fairways require a high degree of precision and a sharp short game. It was designed to provide a more accessible alternative to the championship course, but it has become a favorite in its own right for those looking for a fast, engaging round of links golf.

The Ashludie is often used for junior development and senior tournaments, but it also serves as a tactical warm-up for those heading to the Medal. It shares the same sandy, free-draining soil that makes Monifieth a year-round destination. Notable for its many par-threes and narrow corridors through the gorse, the course rewards the "thinking" golfer over the long hitter. It is a beautifully maintained track that offers all the traditional links challenges on a more compact scale. For many visitors, a day at Monifieth isn't complete without "taking on the Ashludie" after lunch.

Montrose - 1562
Montrose Golf Links is the fifth oldest golf course in the world, with the game having been played on this site since 1562. The "Medal" course was originally laid out by Willie Park Jr. and later refined by James Braid, creating a masterpiece of traditional links design. It is unique for its "T-shaped" routing, which ensures that the wind affects the golfer from every possible angle during the round. It has served as a Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship and remains one of the most respected links in Scotland.

The course is famous for its proximity to the North Sea, with several holes hugging the cliff edge. This beauty comes with a challenge, as coastal erosion is a constant battle for the club. Notable members have included legends of the local community and figures from Scotland's early golfing history. The 1562 course is a sanctuary of deep bunkers, natural dunes, and fast-running turf. To play here is to walk in the footsteps of over four centuries of golfers, offering a historic and spiritually moving connection to the origins of the game.

Montrose - Broomfield
The Broomfield Course at Montrose offers a flatter, more open links experience that is the perfect complement to its historic 1562 neighbor. Originally used as a practice ground for the local militia, the land was developed into a full 18-hole course to meet the growing demand for golf in the town. While it lacks the dramatic elevation changes of the Medal course, it features the same firm, sandy turf and fast greens that characterize the best Scottish links.

Broomfield is particularly popular with families, beginners, and those looking for a walking-friendly round that still provides an authentic links challenge. The wind remains the primary defender of par, and the gorse-lined fairways require accuracy off the tee. It is a municipal-style course in spirit, offering a high-quality playing surface at a very accessible price. Notable for its friendly, relaxed atmosphere, Broomfield ensures that the vast links land of Montrose remains a playground for everyone, from the casual visitor to the scratch amateur looking for a quick, competitive game.

Moray - Old
The Old Course at Moray Golf Club in Lossiemouth is a legendary links designed in 1889 by the "Grand Old Man of Golf" Old Tom Morris. It is a pure, traditional layout that starts and finishes within the town, much like the Old Course at St Andrews. The course is famous for its deep, revetted bunkers, gorse-lined fairways, and its signature finishing hole—a par-four that requires an approach over a steep bank to a green overlooked by the magnificent clubhouse.

The club has a prestigious history and has hosted many of the game's greats, including Sir Henry Cotton, who reportedly loved the challenge of the Moray turf. It has served as a Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship and regularly hosts the Scottish Amateur Championship. One of its most unique features is the proximity to the RAF Lossiemouth airbase; the sight and sound of fighter jets soaring overhead provides a dramatic and modern contrast to the ancient links. Moray Old is a masterclass in classic design, offering a rugged, honest, and intensely atmospheric round of golf.

Moray - New
The New Course at Moray Golf Club is far from a secondary consideration; it is a championship-standard links in its own right. Designed by the legendary Henry Cotton and opened in 1970, it offers a distinct contrast to the Old Tom Morris layout next door. While the Old Course is wide and grand, Cotton's "New" is tighter, more intricate, and arguably more punishing for the wayward driver. It winds through the dense gorse of the Lossiemouth dunes, requiring a strategic "iron-off-the-tee" approach on several holes.

The course is famous for its par-threes, which are often cited as some of the most difficult in the North East, especially when the wind whips off the Moray Firth. Notable for its impeccably fast greens, the New Course frequently hosts the Scottish Ladies' Championship and regional pro-ams. It shares the same dramatic backdrop of RAF Lossiemouth, where the roar of Typhoon jets provides a modern soundtrack to a very traditional links challenge. For many members, the New is the preferred "daily" test because of its relentless demand for accuracy and its beautifully secluded atmosphere within the gorse.

Mormond Hill
Mormond Hill Golf Club is a rugged and scenic 9-hole moorland course located in the heart of Buchan, Aberdeenshire. Established in 1991, the course is built on the slopes of Mormond Hill, famous for its "White Stag" and "White Horse" figures carved into the hillside. The layout is a physical and mental test, featuring significant elevation changes and natural hazards like heather and peat hags. From the higher tees, players are rewarded with panoramic views across the North East toward the sea.

The club is a testament to community spirit, having been founded and maintained by a dedicated group of local enthusiasts. While it doesn't host professional tours, it is a hub for the Buchan Golf Association and is known for its friendly, "no-frills" atmosphere. The greens are small and well-protected by the natural contours of the hill. It is a place where the elements—specifically the wind—dictate the strategy of the day. For those seeking a quiet, authentic Highland-edge experience away from the busy coastal tracks, Mormond Hill offers a peaceful and honest round of golf.

Mount Ellen
Mount Ellen Golf Club, located in Gartcosh near Glasgow, is a delightful 18-hole parkland course that celebrated its centenary in 2004. Originally a 9-hole layout, it was extended to a full 18 in the 1960s. The course is characterized by its lush, tree-lined fairways and its remarkably consistent greens. While it sits near the city, the dense woodland and natural undulations provide a sense of total seclusion. It is a tactical course where position is everything, as several dog-legs and well-placed bunkers punish the overly aggressive.

The club has a proud history of producing high-caliber amateur talent and has a reputation for being one of the friendliest clubs in Lanarkshire. Notable for its excellent drainage, Mount Ellen is often playable when other local courses are struggling with the Scottish rain. The signature 13th hole, "The Pulpit" requires a precise shot from an elevated tee to a green guarded by mature trees. It is a traditional, well-established club that offers a high-quality, polished parkland experience, making it a favorite for local societies and those looking for a refined round near the city.

Muckhart
Muckhart Golf Club is a spectacular 27-hole complex situated at the base of the Ochil Hills in Perthshire. The three 9-hole loops—Cowden, Arndean, and Naemoor—each offer a distinct character, ranging from parkland to moorland. Established in 1908, the club has grown from a modest village course into a premier destination for regional golf. The elevation changes are dramatic, providing views across the Forth Valley to the Wallace Monument and Stirling Castle.

The club is famous for its junior development program, which is widely regarded as one of the best in Scotland. Notable members have included several Scottish internationalists who honed their skills on these tricky, sloping fairways. Because of the three loops, Muckhart offers incredible variety; you can play a different combination of 18 holes every time you visit. The greens are notoriously undulating and fast, requiring a delicate touch. It is a vibrant, modern club that maintains a deep respect for its Highland roots, offering some of the most scenic and engaging golf in the central belt.

Muir of Ord
Muir of Ord Golf Club is a fascinating Highland gem that spans the main Inverness-to-Wick railway line. Originally established as a 9-hole course in 1875, it was extended to 18 holes in the 1920s with input from the legendary James Braid. The course is a moorland-heathland hybrid, characterized by its springy turf, purple heather, and silver birch trees. The railway line comes into play on several holes, requiring a steady nerve as the Highland trains rumble past just yards from the fairway.

The club has a prestigious history and has hosted the Northern Open and various North District championships. One of its most distinctive features is the 18th hole, a par-three that finishes right in front of the clubhouse, providing a dramatic end to the round. The greens at Muir of Ord are famously true and are often cited as some of the best in the Highlands. It is a friendly, community-focused club that offers a historic Braid layout with a unique industrial twist. For any golfer touring the North, the "Muir" provides a charming and strategically rewarding challenge.

Muirfield
Muirfield, the home of The Honourable Company of Edinburgh Golfers, is arguably the most respected and strategically perfect links in the world. Founded in 1744 (though moving to Muirfield in 1891), the course was designed by Old Tom Morris and later refined by Harry Colt. It is unique for its "two-loop" design: the front nine travels clockwise around the perimeter, while the back nine travels counter-clockwise in the center. This ensures the wind attacks the golfer from every possible direction.

Muirfield has hosted The Open Championship 16 times, with winners including Jack Nicklaus (who named his own course, Muirfield Village, after it), Gary Player, Tom Watson, and Phil Mickelson. It is a sanctuary of deep, revetted bunkers and perfectly manicured fairways. The clubhouse is a cathedral of golfing history, and the legendary "Muirfield Lunch" is as famous as the course itself. While it is a private and traditional club, a round at Muirfield is the ultimate bucket-list item for any serious golfer—a masterclass in design, history, and the highest standards of the game.

Murcar Links
Murcar Links is a world-class championship course that sits adjacent to the famous Royal Aberdeen links. Established in 1909 and designed by Archie Simpson, it was later refined by James Braid. It is a rugged, spectacular links characterized by massive sand dunes, deep hollows, and blind shots. Murcar has a significant tournament pedigree, having hosted the European Tour's Saltire Energy Paul Lawrie Matchplay and the Scottish Challenge Tour.


The course is famously difficult, especially when the North Sea gales blow across the exposed dunes. Notable members have included many of the North East's top amateurs. The signature 7th hole, "Serpentine" is a par-four that requires a nerve-wracking drive between towering dunes to a narrowing fairway. Murcar is a "pure" links in every sense—raw, uncompromising, and visually stunning. It offers a more rugged and wild experience than some of its more manicured neighbors, making it a favorite for those who want to experience the true, untamed nature of Scottish coastal golf.

Murrayfield
Murrayfield Golf Club is a prestigious parkland course located in the heart of Edinburgh, offering spectacular views of the city, the Firth of Forth, and the distant mountains of Fife. Established in 1896, the course was significantly improved by the legendary James Braid. Despite its urban location, the course feels remarkably secluded, with its fairways lined by mature trees and lush vegetation. Its most famous feature is the view of Edinburgh Castle from the higher holes on the back nine.

The club has a high-society reputation and has counted many prominent Edinburgh families among its members for generations. It is a tactical course where the elevation changes require careful club selection and a steady hand. Notable for its impeccably fast greens and strategic bunkering, Murrayfield is a regular host for the Lothians County Championships. The clubhouse is a hub of traditional elegance, providing a warm welcome in one of the city's most affluent suburbs. For those looking for a historic, high-quality, and scenic round of golf within minutes of the city center, Murrayfield is a premier choice.

Murrayshall - Murrayshall Course
The Murrayshall Course is the flagship championship layout at the Murrayshall Country Estate near Perth. Designed by Hamilton J. Stutt and opened in 1981, it is a grand, expansive parkland course that winds through the estate's 365 acres of mature woodland and rolling hills. It is a "resort" course of the highest order, featuring wide fairways, large bunkers, and impeccably maintained greens. The course offers spectacular views across the Vale of Strathmore toward the Grampian Mountains.

The course has a strong tournament history, having hosted several PGA Scotland events and the Scottish Schoolboys' Championship. Notable for its "American-style" scale, Murrayshall requires power off the tee but rewards precision on the large, undulating greens. The signature 18th hole, finishing in front of the historic 17th-century manor house, provides a dramatic and luxurious conclusion to the round. It is a polished, professional, and intensely scenic course that offers a five-star golfing experience in the heart of Scotland's most beautiful parkland.

Musselburgh Links - The Old Course
Musselburgh Links is the oldest continuously played golf course in the world, with official records dating back to 1672, though Mary, Queen of Scots, reportedly played here in 1567. This 9-hole course is situated inside the Musselburgh Racecourse and served as a venue for The Open Championship six times between 1874 and 1889. It is the original home of the 4.25-inch golf hole, which was established here when a local member used a hole-cutter made from a piece of drainage pipe.

The course is a living museum, and visitors are encouraged to rent hickory clubs to experience the links as the early pioneers did. Notable winners of the Open here include Park Sr. and Jamie Anderson. The layout is flat and exposed to the sea breeze, featuring historic hazards like "The Grave" bunker. Playing at Musselburgh is a historic rite of passage for any golfer; it is a chance to stand on the same turf where the very foundations of the modern game were laid. It is an unpretentious, spiritually moving, and profoundly important piece of Scotland's national heritage.

Millport
Millport Golf Club, situated on the charming Isle of Cumbrae, offers a classic moorland experience with some of the most panoramic views in the West. Founded in 1888, the course was redesigned in 1913 by the legendary James Braid, who capitalized on the island's natural undulations to create a layout that tests every club in the bag. The course sits high above the town, providing a 360-degree vista that includes the peaks of Arran, the mountains of Argyll, and the Ayrshire coast.


The club has a fiercely loyal membership and a reputation for providing a warm island welcome. While the yardage is modest, Braid's strategic bunkering and the ever-present Firth of Clyde breeze ensure that "mastering" Millport is no easy feat. Notable tournaments include the Cumbrae Open, which draws players from the mainland for a day of competitive golf and ferry-hop adventure.

Milnathort
Milnathort Golf Club is a beautiful 9-hole parkland gem in the heart of Kinross-shire. Established in 1910, the course was refined in the 1920s with input from James Braid. The layout is characterized by its lush, mature trees and the North Queich burn, which meanders through the property and provides a natural hazard on several holes. Despite being 9 holes, it features 18 distinct tee positions to create a varied full-round experience. It is a staple for regional Perth & Kinross county events and is known for its impeccably fast greens.

Milngavie
Milngavie Golf Club is a premier moorland course located on the edge of Mugdock Country Park. Founded in 1895, the course was redesigned in 1923 by James Braid and later refined by Willie Fernie. Set against the dramatic backdrop of the Campsie Fells, it is characterized by heather-lined fairways and natural rock outcrops. It has produced many fine amateur players for Dunbartonshire and is famous for its "hidden" holes and blind shots that provide a sense of adventure rare in modern parkland golf.

Minto
Minto Golf Club is a scenic parkland course situated on the slopes of Minto Hill in the Scottish Borders. Established in 1928 and designed by local professional Tom Fernie, the course rewards accuracy and stamina with dramatic elevation changes. It offers panoramic views across the Teviot Valley toward the Cheviot Hills. Known for its warm Borders hospitality, Minto is a regular venue for Borders Golfers' Association tournaments and features the signature "Pulpit" hole (the 12th).

Moffat
Moffat Golf Club is a rugged and atmospheric moorland course offering a unique "highland" experience in the Southern Uplands. Founded in 1884, it was redesigned in 1904 by the legendary Ben Sayers. Sitting 800 feet above sea level, it overlooks the Annan Valley. The club is famous for its "volcanic" greens—small, plateau surfaces that require surgical precision. It relies on natural terrain, heather, and wind rather than artificial bunkers to defend its par.


Monifieth - Medal
The Medal Course at Monifieth Links is a titan of Angus golf and a frequent Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship. With a history dating back to 1639, the course was refined by Old Tom Morris and James Braid. It is famously the place where Tom Watson played his first-ever round of links golf before winning the 1975 Open. It has hosted almost every major amateur championship in Scotland and remains a favorite for low-handicappers seeking a pure links test.

Monifieth - Ashludie
The Ashludie Course is the Medal's shorter, quirkier sibling. While measuring just over 5,000 yards, its small, undulating greens and tight fairways require high precision. It shares the same sandy, free-draining soil as the championship track and is a favorite for those looking for a fast, engaging round. It is often used for junior development and senior tournaments, rewarding "thinking" golf over raw power.

Montrose - 1562
Montrose Golf Links is the fifth oldest golf course in the world, with golf played here since 1562. The "Medal" course was originally laid out by Willie Park Jr. and refined by James Braid. It is unique for its "T-shaped" routing, ensuring the wind affects the golfer from every angle. It has served as a Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship and remains one of the most respected links in Scotland, though it faces a constant battle with coastal erosion.


Montrose - Broomfield
The Broomfield Course at Montrose offers a flatter, more open links experience. Originally used as a practice ground for the local militia, it was developed into a full 18-hole course to meet demand. It is particularly popular with families and beginners, offering an authentic links challenge at a very accessible price. The wind remains the primary defender of par, and the gorse-lined fairways require accuracy off the tee.

Moray - Old
The Old Course at Moray Golf Club in Lossiemouth is a legendary links designed in 1889 by Old Tom Morris. Like St Andrews, it starts and finishes within the town. It is famous for its deep, revetted bunkers and its signature finishing hole overlooked by the magnificent clubhouse. It has hosted legends like Sir Henry Cotton and regularly holds the Scottish Amateur Championship. The sight and sound of RAF Typhoon jets from the nearby base provide a dramatic, modern contrast to the ancient links.

Moray - New
The New Course at Moray Golf Club is far from a secondary consideration; it is a championship-standard links in its own right. Designed by the legendary Henry Cotton and opened in 1970, it offers a distinct contrast to the Old Tom Morris layout next door. While the Old Course is wide and grand, Cotton's "New" is tighter, more intricate, and arguably more punishing for the wayward driver. It winds through the dense gorse of the Lossiemouth dunes, requiring a strategic "iron-off-the-tee" approach on several holes.

The course is famous for its par-threes, which are often cited as some of the most difficult in the North East, especially when the wind whips off the Moray Firth. Notable for its impeccably fast greens, the New Course frequently hosts the Scottish Ladies' Championship and regional pro-ams. It shares the same dramatic backdrop of RAF Lossiemouth, where the roar of Typhoon jets provides a modern soundtrack to a very traditional links challenge. For many members, the New is the preferred "daily" test because of its relentless demand for accuracy and its beautifully secluded atmosphere within the gorse.

Mortonhall
Founded in 1892, Mortonhall Golf Club is the oldest parkland course in the City of Edinburgh. The current layout is largely the work of Fred Hawtree, who in the 1970s transformed the site into a lush, premier parkland test. The course is situated on the historic Mortonhall Estate, and its fairways are lined with spectacular mature trees and vibrant gorse, offering a sense of total isolation despite being just four miles from the city center.

The club has a prestigious history and has produced many fine amateur players, including former Walker Cup participants. It is a regular host for the Lothians County Championships and is widely regarded as one of the best-conditioned inland courses in Scotland. The views from the higher holes toward the Pentland Hills and the Firth of Forth are cinematic. Mortonhall is a "pure" golf club that prioritizes the quality of its turf and the speed of its greens. It offers a refined, aristocratic experience that bridges the gap between the wild links of the coast and the manicured estates of the capital.

Muckhart
Muckhart Golf Club is a spectacular 27-hole complex situated at the base of the Ochil Hills in Perthshire. The three 9-hole loops—Cowden, Arndean, and Naemoor—each offer a distinct character, ranging from parkland to moorland. Established in 1908, the club has grown from a modest village course into a premier destination for regional golf. The elevation changes are dramatic, providing views across the Forth Valley to the Wallace Monument and Stirling Castle.

The club is famous for its junior development program, which is widely regarded as one of the best in Scotland. Notable members have included several Scottish internationalists who honed their skills on these tricky, sloping fairways. Because of the three loops, Muckhart offers incredible variety; you can play a different combination of 18 holes every time you visit. The greens are notoriously undulating and fast, requiring a delicate touch. It is a vibrant, modern club that maintains a deep respect for its Highland roots, offering some of the most scenic and engaging golf in the central belt.

Muirfield
Muirfield, the home of The Honourable Company of Edinburgh Golfers, is arguably the most respected and strategically perfect links in the world. Founded in 1744, the course was designed by Old Tom Morris and later refined by Harry Colt. It is unique for its "two-loop" design: the front nine travels clockwise around the perimeter, while the back nine travels counter-clockwise in the center. This ensures the wind attacks the golfer from every possible direction throughout the round.

Muirfield has hosted The Open Championship 16 times, with winners including Jack Nicklaus (who named his own course, Muirfield Village, after it), Gary Player, Tom Watson, and Phil Mickelson. It is a sanctuary of deep, revetted bunkers and perfectly manicured fairways. The clubhouse is a cathedral of golfing history, and the legendary "Muirfield Lunch" is as famous as the course itself. While it is a private and traditional club, a round at Muirfield is the ultimate bucket-list item for any serious golfer—a masterclass in design and the highest standards of the game.

Muirkirk
Muirkirk Golf Club is a rugged and scenic 9-hole moorland course tucked away in the East Ayrshire hills. Established in 1905, the course is a "hidden gem" in the truest sense, offering a raw and authentic Scottish golfing experience. The layout is set on the slopes of the Garpel Valley, providing panoramic views of the surrounding countryside. It is a natural course where the fairways follow the undulating contours of the land, and the hazards are largely provided by the heather, gorse, and the local burn.

The club is known for its warm, unpretentious welcome and its resilient community spirit. While it lacks a "celebrity" architect, the clever routing makes excellent use of the natural slopes to create a variety of challenging shots. It is a place where the elements—specifically the wind—dictate the strategy of the day. The course is famously well-maintained by a small, dedicated team, and the greens are often surprisingly true for such an exposed site. For those seeking a quiet, traditional round of golf away from the crowds, Muirkirk offers a peaceful and honest connection to the game's roots.

Muir of Ord
Muir of Ord Golf Club is a fascinating Highland gem that spans the main Inverness-to-Wick railway line. Originally established as a 9-hole course in 1875, it was extended to 18 holes in the 1920s with input from the legendary James Braid. The course is a moorland-heathland hybrid, characterized by its springy turf, purple heather, and silver birch trees. The railway line comes into play on several holes, requiring a steady nerve as the Highland trains rumble past just yards from the fairway.

The club has a prestigious history and has hosted the Northern Open and various North District championships. One of its most distinctive features is the 18th hole, a par-three that finishes right in front of the clubhouse, providing a dramatic end to the round. The greens at Muir of Ord are famously true and are often cited as some of the best in the Highlands. It is a friendly, community-focused club that offers a historic Braid layout with a unique industrial twist. For any golfer touring the North, the "Muir" provides a charming and strategically rewarding challenge.

Murcar
Murcar Links is a world-class championship course that sits adjacent to the famous Royal Aberdeen. Established in 1909 and designed by Archie Simpson, it was later refined by James Braid. It is a rugged, spectacular links characterized by massive sand dunes, deep hollows, and blind shots. Murcar has a significant tournament pedigree, having hosted the European Tour's Saltire Energy Paul Lawrie Matchplay and the Scottish Challenge Tour.


The course is famously difficult, especially when the North Sea gales blow across the exposed dunes. Notable members have included many of the North East's top amateurs. The signature 7th hole, "Serpentine" is a par-four that requires a nerve-wracking drive between towering dunes to a narrowing fairway. Murcar is a "pure" links in every sense—raw, uncompromising, and visually stunning. It offers a more rugged and wild experience than some of its more manicured neighbors, making it a favorite for those who want to experience the true, untamed nature of Scottish coastal golf.

Murcar - Strabathie
The Strabathie Course at Murcar is the 9-hole sibling to the championship links, but it should not be underestimated. Originally intended as a training ground for the main course, it has evolved into a high-quality links-parkland hybrid that offers a quicker but still testing round of golf. The layout features many of the same natural hazards as the main course, including deep bunkers and thick gorse, but on a slightly more compact and accessible scale.

Strabathie is particularly popular with juniors, beginners, and those looking for a fast "nine" after work. It is maintained to the same exacting standards as the championship links, ensuring a world-class playing surface throughout the year. While it lacks the international spotlight of the main course, it provides a vital entry point for new golfers in the Aberdeen area. It is a delightful, walking-friendly track that allows players to experience the unique turf and sea breezes of Murcar without the punishing length of the full championship experience.

Murrayfield
Murrayfield Golf Club is a prestigious parkland course located in the heart of Edinburgh, offering spectacular views of the city, the Firth of Forth, and the distant mountains of Fife. Established in 1896, the course was significantly improved by the legendary James Braid. Despite its urban location, the course feels remarkably secluded, with its fairways lined by mature trees and lush vegetation. Its most famous feature is the view of Edinburgh Castle from the higher holes on the back nine.

The club has a high-society reputation and has counted many prominent Edinburgh families among its members for generations. It is a tactical course where the elevation changes require careful club selection and a steady hand. Notable for its impeccably fast greens and strategic bunkering, Murrayfield is a regular host for the Lothians County Championships. The clubhouse is a hub of traditional elegance, providing a warm welcome in one of the city's most affluent suburbs. For those looking for a historic, high-quality round within minutes of the city center, Murrayfield is a premier choice.

Murrayshall
The Murrayshall Course is the flagship championship layout at the Murrayshall Country Estate near Perth. Designed by Hamilton J. Stutt and opened in 1981, it is a grand, expansive parkland course that winds through the estate's 365 acres of mature woodland and rolling hills. It is a "resort" course of the highest order, featuring wide fairways, large bunkers, and impeccably maintained greens. The course offers spectacular views across the Vale of Strathmore toward the Grampian Mountains.

The course has a strong tournament history, having hosted several PGA Scotland events and the Scottish Schoolboys' Championship. Notable for its "American-style" scale, Murrayshall requires power off the tee but rewards precision on the large, undulating greens. The signature 18th hole, finishing in front of the historic 17th-century manor house, provides a dramatic and luxurious conclusion to the round. It is a polished, professional, and intensely scenic course that offers a five-star golfing experience in the heart of Scotland's most beautiful parkland.

Musselburgh
Musselburgh Golf Club, also known as Monktonhall, is a prestigious parkland course designed in 1938 by the legendary James Braid. While it is often confused with the historic Old Links nearby, Monktonhall is a distinct championship-standard layout that serves as a Final Qualifying venue for the Open Championship. Braid utilized the naturally undulating terrain to create a course that emphasizes strategic positioning, featuring his signature deep bunkers and well-protected greens.

The club has a prestigious history and has produced many fine amateur players, often featuring in the top tiers of the Lothians County circuit. Famous visitors have included numerous pros during Open qualifying weeks who have praised the course for its consistency and "fair" but firm challenge. The layout is beautifully tree-lined, providing a sense of seclusion despite its proximity to Edinburgh. The signature 18th hole, requiring a precise approach to a green nestled near the clubhouse, is one of the finest finishing holes in the region. It remains a "pure" golf club that prioritizes the quality of its turf and the traditions of the game.

Musselburgh Old Links
The Old Links at Musselburgh is one of the most significant sites in the history of the sport. It is the oldest continuously played golf course in the world, with records dating back to 1672. Situated inside the Musselburgh Racecourse, it served as a venue for The Open Championship six times between 1874 and 1889. It is the birthplace of the standard 4.25-inch golf hole, which was first established here using a piece of drainage pipe as a hole-cutter.

The course is a living museum, and many visitors choose to rent hickory clubs to play the nine holes as the early pioneers did. Notable Open winners here include Mungo Park and Jamie Anderson. The layout is flat and exposed, with historic hazards like "The Grave" bunker providing a stern test of nerves. Playing Musselburgh Old is a spiritual experience for any golfer, offering a direct connection to the 19th-century "Great Triumvirate" era. It is an unpretentious, historic treasure that remains a mandatory pilgrimage for anyone interested in the roots of Scottish golf.

Muthill
Muthill Golf Club is a charming and scenic 9-hole parkland course located near Crieff in Perthshire. Established in 1946, the course is perched on a hillside, offering panoramic views over the Strathearn Valley toward the Grampian Mountains. While it is a shorter course, Muthill is notorious for its sloping fairways and small, tricky greens that require a delicate touch and precise club selection. It is a "mountain-lite" experience that tests your fitness as much as your finesse.

The club is known for its warm, community-focused atmosphere and is a favorite for local society outings. Although it doesn't host professional tours, it has a reputation for being one of the best-maintained 9-hole tracks in the county. The 2nd hole, "The Whinny" is a highlight, requiring a blind shot over a ridge to a hidden green. Muthill represents the quintessential Scottish village club—friendly, unpretentious, and deeply integrated into the local landscape. For those looking for a quick, scenic, and technically demanding round, it offers high-value golf in a truly tranquil setting.

Nairn Dunbar
Nairn Dunbar Golf Links is a heavyweight of Northern golf, frequently overshadowed by its neighbor but a formidable championship test in its own right. Founded in 1899, the course has evolved into a rugged links-heathland hybrid. In recent years, a major restoration project returned the course to its "pure" links roots by removing gorse to reveal natural sand dunes. It has a high tournament pedigree, having hosted the British Amateur Championship and the Scottish Boys' Championship.

The course is famous for its brutal opening stretch and its remarkably fast, true greens. Notable members have included several Walker Cup players, and the club prides itself on a competitive yet welcoming environment. Unlike some traditional links, Nairn Dunbar offers a variety of holes that wind through both dunes and more sheltered, tree-lined areas. The wind off the Moray Firth is a constant defender of par, making a low-ball flight essential. It is a polished, professional club that offers a world-class links experience with a slightly more modern and relaxed feel than the ancient coastal giants.

Nairn
The Nairn Golf Club is a global masterpiece and a jewel of the Moray Firth. Established in 1887 and refined by legends including Old Tom Morris, James Braid, and Ben Sayers, it is a pure links of the highest order. It is one of the few courses in the world where the sea is visible from every single hole. Nairn has an immense international reputation, famously hosting the 1999 Walker Cup and the 2012 Curtis Cup, where the quality of its firm, fast fairways drew worldwide acclaim.

The course is renowned for having some of the best greens in Scotland—lightning-fast and remarkably smooth. Famous members and visitors include royalty and major champions who seek out its technical complexity. The first seven holes hug the shoreline so closely that a stray hook will literally land in the North Sea. It is a traditional, aristocratic club where the standards of play and etiquette are held in the highest regard. To play Nairn is to experience the absolute pinnacle of links architecture—a beautiful, unforgiving, and spiritually uplifting journey across the Highland coastline.

Nairn - Cameron
The Cameron Course at Nairn is the 9-hole sibling to the famous championship links, offering a high-quality "warm-up" or a more relaxed alternative for families and beginners. Despite its shorter length, the Cameron is maintained by the same world-class greenkeeping team as the main course, ensuring the same fast-running turf and immaculate greens. It consists of a mix of par-threes and par-fours that provide a tactical challenge without the intimidating length of the championship track.

The Cameron is particularly popular for junior development and for members looking for a quick "nine" after work. It offers a wonderful view of the Moray Firth and allows players to sample the unique Nairn microclimate and turf. While it lacks the international accolades of its elder brother, it is a vital part of the club's identity, providing an accessible gateway to one of the world's great golfing venues. It is the perfect example of how a premier club can cater to all levels of the game while maintaining an uncompromising standard of quality.

Newbattle
Newbattle Golf Club is a hidden parkland gem located on the outskirts of Dalkeith, just south of Edinburgh. Founded in 1933, the course is set within the historic estate of Newbattle Abbey, and its fairways wind through ancient woodland and along the banks of the River Esk. The layout is characterized by its lush, mature trees and several significant elevation changes, providing a peaceful and sheltered environment that feels miles away from the nearby capital.

The club has a prestigious history in the Lothians and is known for its competitive amateur teams. The course is a tactical test where the river comes into play on several of the most challenging holes, requiring a steady nerve on the approach shots. Notable for its fast, sloping greens, Newbattle is a regular host for regional county events. The 13th hole, "The Maiden" is a scenic par-three that requires a precise shot over a valley. It is a friendly, traditional club that offers a refined and atmospheric round of golf, where the history of the abbey estate adds a touch of grandeur to every shot.

Newburgh on Ythan
Newburgh on Ythan Golf Club is a spectacular links course situated at the mouth of the River Ythan in Aberdeenshire. Originally a 9-hole course founded in 1888, it was extended to a full 18 holes in the 1990s. The course is a "tale of two halves": the front nine offers a more modern, hilly links feel with expansive views, while the back nine winds through massive, ancient dunes that are among the most dramatic in the country. It sits adjacent to the Forvie National Nature Reserve, making it a haven for wildlife.

The course is famous for its demanding elevation changes and the blind shots required through the dunes. While it lacks a "celebrity" architect, the natural routing is a masterclass in links design. Notable for its firm turf and punishing rough, Newburgh is a favorite for those who enjoy the "raw" and unadulterated side of links golf. The views from the 4th tee, overlooking the estuary and the North Sea, are worth the green fee alone. It is a welcoming, community-led club that offers a wild and memorable challenge that rivals the more famous links of the North East.

Newcastleton
Newcastleton Golf Club is a rugged and charming 9-hole moorland course situated in the heart of the Liddesdale Valley in the Scottish Borders. Established in 1910, the course is perched on the slopes of Holm Hill, offering panoramic views over the village and the surrounding border hills. It is a "natural" course in the truest sense, where the fairways follow the steep contours of the land and the primary hazards are provided by the heather, gorse, and the local topography.

The club is an "honesty box" facility, maintained with immense pride by a small group of local volunteers. While it doesn't host professional tournaments, it is a beloved part of the local community and offers a peaceful, solitary experience for visiting golfers. The layout requires a good level of fitness due to the steep climbs, but the reward is a series of exhilarating elevated tee shots. Newcastleton is a place where you can escape the pressures of modern life and experience the game as it was played over a century ago—simple, beautiful, and deeply connected to the landscape.

New Cumnock
New Cumnock Golf Club is a scenic 9-hole parkland course located at the foot of the Afton Hills in East Ayrshire. Founded in 1902, the course is situated on the banks of the River Nith, which provides a natural and often treacherous hazard on several holes. The layout is relatively flat, making it an enjoyable walk, but the narrow fairways and well-placed trees require a high degree of accuracy. The backdrop of the hills and the tranquility of the river make it one of the most peaceful spots in the county.

The club is a pillar of the local community and is known for its warm, unpretentious hospitality. While it lacks a "famous name" architect, the course is highly regarded for its conditioning and the quality of its greens. It is a regular venue for Ayrshire regional amateur events and has a reputation for being a "friendly" course that still offers a stiff challenge to low-handicappers. The signature 5th hole, playing right along the riverbank, is a highlight of the round. New Cumnock offers an authentic and high-value taste of Ayrshire parkland golf in a stunning rural setting.

New Galloway
New Galloway Golf Club is a captivating 9-hole gem located in the heart of the Galloway Forest Park. Established in 1902, the course is a rugged moorland layout that offers some of the most stunning views in the South West. It is famously undulating—many locals joke that there isn't a flat lie on the entire property—forcing players to adapt their stance and shot-making constantly. The course is surrounded by ancient woodland and is a haven for red kites and deer.


The club is a focal point of Scotland's smallest royal burgh and is known for its incredibly warm, inclusive atmosphere. While it doesn't boast a celebrity architect, the routing is a masterpiece of natural design, utilizing rocky outcrops and heather as organic hazards. The signature 6th hole, "The Lairig" requires a nerve-wracking shot over a valley to a green perched on a hillside. New Galloway offers a peaceful, "slow-golf" experience where the beauty of the landscape is just as important as the score on the card.

Newmachar - Hawkshill
The Hawkshill Course at Newmachar is a titan of North East golf. Designed by the renowned Dave Thomas and opened in 1990, it was built to be a premier championship test. It is a dense, tree-lined parkland course that demands total precision; many holes are framed by tight corridors of Scots pine and silver birch. Hawkshill has a serious tournament pedigree, having hosted the European Challenge Tour and various prestigious PGA Scotland events.


The course is notorious for its difficult par-fives and its slick, subtly undulating greens. Notable members include several of Aberdeen's top professional and amateur players who use Hawkshill's unforgiving layout to hone their skills. The signature 18th hole, with water guarding the green right in front of the clubhouse, has seen many dramatic finishes in professional competition. It is a polished, professional, and physically demanding course that offers a "Tour-level" experience for those who think they can handle the heat.

Newmachar - Swailend
The Swailend Course is Newmachar's slightly more "user-friendly" sibling, but it is a formidable challenge in its own right. Also designed by Dave Thomas and opened in 1997, Swailend is more open than the Hawkshill, allowing for a bit more freedom off the tee. However, the greens are arguably more difficult, featuring significant tiers and slopes that require a master's touch with the putter. It offers a more rolling, traditional parkland feel compared to the dense forest of its neighbor.

Swailend is a favorite for local club medals and society outings because it offers a high-quality playing surface without being quite as "penal" as the Hawkshill. It is meticulously maintained by the same world-class greenkeeping team, ensuring year-round conditioning that is among the best in the region. Notable for its scenic views over the Aberdeenshire countryside, Swailend provides a perfect balance of challenge and enjoyment, making the Newmachar complex one of the most comprehensive golfing destinations in the country.

Newtonmore
Newtonmore Golf Club is a spectacular Highland course that sits on the banks of the River Spey within the Cairngorms National Park. Established in 1893, the course is mostly flat—a rarity for a Highland track—making it an easy and enjoyable walk. It is a parkland-moorland hybrid that follows the meandering path of the river, which acts as a beautiful but deadly hazard on several holes. The mountain scenery surrounding the course is truly breathtaking.

The club is famous for its connection to the local community and its long-standing friendly rivalry with neighboring Kingussie. While it lacks a "famous" architect, the layout is highly regarded for its logical flow and the trueness of its greens. It has hosted various North District events and is a regular favorite for visiting golfers touring the Spey Valley. The 18th hole, "The Home Hole" requires an approach over a burn to a green overlooked by the traditional clubhouse. Newtonmore offers a tranquil and authentically Highland round of golf that emphasizes the natural beauty of the Spey.

Newton Stewart
Newton Stewart Golf Club is a scenic parkland beauty located on the edge of the Galloway Hills. Founded in 1896, the course offers panoramic views of the "Cairnsmore of Fleet" and the surrounding countryside. The layout is characterized by its varied terrain, ranging from gentle slopes to significant elevation changes that require a creative approach. It is a beautifully wooded course, with mature trees framing the fairways and providing shelter from the Dumfries and Galloway breezes.

The club is known for its warm hospitality and its well-conditioned greens. While it may not have hosted the professional majors, it is a stalwart of the South of Scotland amateur circuit. The signature 9th hole, "The Monument" is a highlight, played toward the local memorial landmark on the hill. Newton Stewart is a traditional, unpretentious club that rewards accuracy over power. It is an ideal stop for those exploring the South West, offering a peaceful and engaging round of golf in a setting that captures the lush, green essence of the Galloway landscape.

Niddry Castle
Niddry Castle Golf Club is a unique parkland course located in Winchburgh, West Lothian. The course is built around the historic 15th-century Niddry Castle, where Mary, Queen of Scots once sought refuge. Opened in the 1980s, the layout was designed to incorporate the castle as a dramatic focal point. It is a challenging course characterized by its significant elevation changes, water hazards, and tight, tree-lined fairways that require careful strategic planning.

The club is a vibrant community hub and is known for its friendly, modern atmosphere. While it is a relatively young course, it has matured quickly and is a regular host for West Lothian County events. The signature hole is the par-three over the pond toward the castle, providing one of the most iconic views in Lothian golf. Niddry Castle is a "thinking man's" course where the driver is often left in the bag in favor of accuracy. It offers a memorable blend of historic grandeur and modern parkland challenges, making it a standout in the central belt.

North Berwick
The West Links at North Berwick is a global icon and a masterpiece of traditional links design. Founded in 1832, the course is a pure links that starts and finishes in the heart of the town.
